Refutation of the Knowledge Condition: Tafsil
Refutes the condition of knowledge by disjunction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator resolves the objection regarding the requirement of knowledge for obligation by distinguishing conception, belief, and certain belief.

Core Idea
The term 'knowledge' in the objection is equivocal; each meaning leads to a different denial.

An objection is raised: if the heedless is defined as one who does not know the obligation, then why exempt the second type? The commentator answers by disambiguating 'knowledge' into conception, belief, and certain belief, showing that the objection fails in each case.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection claims that the definition of the heedless should exclude the second type if 'knowledge' is required.
The objector uses 'knowledge' ambiguously.
2
Follow the disjunction
The commentator analyzes three possible meanings of 'knowledge': conception, belief, and certain belief.
For each, either the minor or major premise is denied.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The objection fails because heedlessness of conception is the only relevant factor; the technical term 'ghafil' refers to that.
The commentator's answer is comprehensive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب أن الغافل الذي لا يجوز تكليفه إجماعاً من لا يفهم الخطاب أصلاً كالصبي والمجنون أو يفهم ذلك ولكن لم يقل له إنك مكلف كالذي لم تبلغه دعوة نبي قطعا فإن هذين غافلان عن تصور التكليف بالتنبيه عليه فلا تكليف على الأول اتفاقاً، ولا على الثاني عندنا لا من لا يعلم أنه مكلف مع أنه خوطب بكونه مكلفاً حال ما كان الثاني فاهما، فإنه غافل عن التصديق بالتكليف لا عن تصوره، وذلك لا يمنع من تكليفه وإلا لم يكن الكفار مكلفين إذ ليسوا مصدقين بالتكليف. قوله : ( أن الغافل (الخ يعني أن الغافل الذي حكم عليه بأنه لا يجوز تكليفه إجماعاً له، فرد أن أحدهما متفق عليه والآخر مختل فيه والإجماع على الحكم باعتبار عنوان الغافل لا ينافي الاختلاف في صدقه على بعض الأشياء، فلا ينافي قيد الإجماع بقوله، ولا على الثاني عندنا وما قيل : إن المراد أن الغافل الذي لا يجوز تكليفه إجماعاً لا يخرج عن أحد المذكورين، لأن كلاً منهما لا يجوز تكليفه إجماعاً حتى ينافيه فلا يخفى ركاكته إذ المحكوم عليه بعدم الجواز إجماعاً ليس إلا الواحد المعين، فلا فائدة لضم النوع الآخر إليه، والحكم على سبيل الإبهام. قوله : ( فإنه غافل الخ) أشار بذلك إلى أن الجواب بالترديد وحاصله أنه إن أريد بالعلم في قوله : لا يعلم التكليف التصور نمنع الصغرى أعني قوله : لأن من لا يعلم هذه الأمور لا يعلم التكليف، لأن تصور التكليف لا يتوقف على التصديق بالأمور المذكورة وإن أريد به التصديق نمنع الكبرى أعني قوله : وإذا لم يعلم التكليف أي لم يصدق به كان غافلاً، فإن الغافل من لا يفهمه أو لم يقل له : إنك مكلف وإن أريد به التصديق اليقيني كما هو اللازم من الحد المختار، فدائرة البحث أوسع لجواز أن يكون ظانا بالتكليف أو مقلداً به، وإنما لم يقيد الشارح التصديق باليقيني لأن الغافل في الاصطلاح هو الغافل عن التصور.
And the answer is that the heedless whose obligation is not permissible by consensus is one who does not understand the address at all, like a child or a madman, or one who understands it but has not been told that he is obligated, like one whom a prophet’s call has absolutely not reached. For these two are heedless of the conception of obligation by way of attention to it; so there is no obligation upon the first by agreement, nor upon the second according to us. But [the heedless who is exempt is] not one who does not know that he is obligated despite being addressed as obligated while he is understanding; for such a one is heedless of the belief in obligation, not of its conception, and that does not prevent his obligation. Otherwise, the unbelievers would not be obligated, since they are not believers in obligation. His statement: (that the heedless…) means that the heedless who is judged that his obligation is not permissible by consensus has two individuals: one agreed upon and the other disputed, and the consensus on the judgment by virtue of the title “heedless” does not negate the disagreement about its applicability to some things, so it does not negate the restriction of consensus by his statement: “nor upon the second according to us.” And what is said: that the intended meaning is that the heedless whose obligation is not permissible by consensus does not fall outside one of the two mentioned, because each of them is not permissible to obligate by consensus until it contradicts, is not hidden in its weakness, because the one judged as not permissible by consensus is only the specific one, so there is no benefit in joining the other type to it and judging vaguely. His statement: (for he is heedless…) he indicated by that that the answer is by way of disjunction, and its summary is that if by “knowledge” in his statement “he does not know the obligation” is meant conception, we deny the minor premise, namely his statement: “because whoever does not know these things does not know the obligation,” because the conception of obligation does not depend on belief in the mentioned things. And if by it is meant belief, we deny the major premise, namely his statement: “and when he does not know the obligation, i.e., does not believe in it, he is heedless,” for the heedless is one who does not understand it or has not been told: “you are obligated.” And if by it is meant certain belief, as is necessary from the chosen definition, then the scope of inquiry is wider, because it is possible that he is assuming the obligation or following it blindly. And the commentator did not restrict belief to certain because the heedless in terminology is the one heedless of conception.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage defines who qualifies as 'al-ghafil' (the heedless) whose obligation is unanimously impermissible. The author distinguishes two types: one who does not understand the address at all (like a child or madman), and one who understands but has never been informed of obligation (like someone unreached by a prophet). Both are heedless of the very concept of obligation. The first is exempt by consensus; the second is exempt according to the author's school. However, the person who does understand the address and has been told he is obligated, yet does not believe in the obligation, is not truly heedless of the concept; he is only heedless of belief (tasdiq). This does not remove his obligation, otherwise unbelievers would not be obligated. The commentary explains that the objection based on consensus does not undermine the distinction: consensus applies only to the first type, not the second. The commentator also resolves a potential equivocation in the term 'knowledge': if it means conception, the minor premise is denied; if it means belief, the major premise is denied; if it means certain belief, the argument does not apply to those with mere opinion or taqlid. The key nuance is that the technical term 'ghafil' in kalam refers to heedlessness of conception, not of belief.

Key Terms
التصور
التصديق
اليقين
التقليد
الظن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator’s disjunction, if 'knowledge' in the objection means belief (tasdiq), which premise is denied?
A) The minor premise
B) The major premise
C) Both premises
D) Neither premise

Key Takeaway
The objection against the definition of the heedless fails because the term 'knowledge' is ambiguous; proper disambiguation resolves it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the word 'knowledge' in religious discussions, do you instinctively equate it with certainty? How has this lesson exposed the danger of such an assumption in your own thinking about obligations? Are you willing to pause and clarify which meaning is intended before passing judgment?
?
The passage shows that a key objection was resolved by disambiguating a term. Think of a time when you misunderstood a religious ruling because you assumed a meaning without checking. What would it require for you to habitually seek clarity on technical terms in Islamic texts before concluding?

Circularity Argument Against Knowledge Condition
Proves that conditioning obligation on knowledge of it leads to circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why conditioning obligation on knowledge of the obligation leads to a vicious circle, and why alternative proposals like conjecture or taqlid fail to resolve it.

Core Idea
Obligation cannot depend on knowledge of itself without falling into circularity.

Theologians debated whether a person must know they are obligated before the obligation takes effect. Al-Jurjani presents a powerful rational argument: knowledge of an event is a shadow of the event itself, so conditioning obligation on prior knowledge would create a logical circle. This lesson unpacks that argument and examines the responses it provoked.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Note the key claim: 'knowledge is a shadow of the occurrence'.
The phrase 'ظل لوقوعه' is a classic expression of epistemological dependency.
2
Identify the circularity
Trace the reasoning: X (knowledge) depends on Y (obligation). If Y depends on X, then X depends on Y which depends on X, etc. That is the circle.
The commentator calls this the 'second proof'.
3
Consider objections and responses
The passage includes an objection by al-Jahiz and the author's rebuttal, plus further objections about conjecture, taqlid, and necessity. Think about whether these objections are persuasive.
The commentator dismisses al-Jahiz's objection as irrelevant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأن العلم بوقوع التكليف موقوف على وقوعه فإن العلم بوقوع شيء ظل لوقوعه في نفسه فلو توقف وقوعه على العلم والتصديق به لزم الدور. قوله : ( عطف على ما تقدم الخ ) فهو دليل ثان لقوله لا من لا يعلم أنه مكلف، وحاصله أن اشتراطه يستلزم الدور، فالجواب بأن مراد الجاحظ أن الموقوف عليه لوقوع التكليف، يتوقف على وقوع التكليف فلا دور كلام لا مساس له أصلاً . يشير إليه بقوله ولأن العلم بوقوع التكليف إلى آخره فيرد أنه لم لا يكفي الظن أو التقليد ؟ وأيضاً الضرورة لا تستلزم الحصول، فيلزم أن لا يكلف من لم يحصل له ما يتوقف عليه التكليف، فإن قلت : إذا كان الموقوف عليه ضرورياً يكفي للتكليف التنبيه عليه، قلت : له عدم الإصغاء حينئذ فتأمل .
And because knowledge of the occurrence of obligation depends on its occurrence, for knowledge of the occurrence of a thing is a shadow of its occurrence in itself. So if its occurrence were dependent on knowledge and belief of it, circularity would follow. His statement: (it is coordinated with what preceded) so it is a second proof for his statement: “not one who does not know that he is obligated,” and its summary is that conditioning it entails circularity. So the answer that what al-Jahiz meant is that what the occurrence of obligation depends on is dependent on the occurrence of obligation, so there is no circularity, is speech that has no bearing at all. He indicates by his statement “and because knowledge of the occurrence of obligation” etc., and it is objected: why is conjecture or taqlid not sufficient? Also, necessity does not necessitate obtaining, so it would follow that one for whom what obligation depends on has not been obtained is not obligated. If you say: if what it depends on is necessary, then reminding is sufficient for obligation, I say: then he has no heed at that time, so reflect.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof against the condition that knowledge of obligation is required for obligation itself. The matn states: knowledge of the occurrence of obligation depends on the actual occurrence of obligation itself, because knowledge follows the known. If obligation's occurrence were conditioned on knowledge/belief of it, a vicious circle (dawr) would result. The commentary explains that this is a second proof (the first being the definition of the heedless). It refutes a potential objection attributed to al-Jahiz: that what obligation depends on is the belief in the possibility of obligation, which itself does not depend on actual obligation. The commentator dismisses this as irrelevant because the proof is about knowledge of the occurrence, not possibility. The commentary then raises further issues: why not suffice with conjecture or taqlid? Also, if the precondition is necessary, merely reminding might still fail if the person does not listen. The argument firmly establishes that obligation cannot be logically preceded by knowledge of itself.

Key Terms
الدور
العلم بوقوع التكليف
الظل
الظن
التقليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the circularity argument can be easily avoided by redefining the precondition (e.g., as belief in possibility rather than actual knowledge).
Correction: The commentator shows that the proof specifically targets knowledge of the occurrence, not just any belief, so such redefinitions miss the point.

Checkpoint
What logical problem arises if obligation depends on knowledge of obligation?
A) Infinite regress.
B) Circularity (dawr).
C) Contradiction.
D) Incoherence.

Key Takeaway
Conditioning obligation on prior knowledge of itself leads to a logical circle, because knowledge of an occurring event depends on the event itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that conditioning obligation on prior knowledge leads to circularity. Think about a duty you have been postponing—whether toward Allah or others—because you keep saying 'I need to know more first.' How is that excuse actually a form of circular reasoning that keeps you from ever starting?
?
The commentator dismisses al-Jahiz's objection as irrelevant. Consider your own heart: Are there times when you redefine what is required of you to avoid the clear call of obligation? What would it mean to stop avoiding and simply respond to the obligation as it is?
?
The passage ends with 'then he has no heed at that time, so reflect.' When has your own lack of heed—your turning away from a reminder—been the real barrier, not a lack of knowledge? What will you do today to break that pattern?

Intuitive Knowledge with Implicit Proofs
The commentator answers the objection that the three necessary propositions are theoretical, not intuitive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the three necessary propositions remain intuitive (badīhiyya) even though they are supported by proofs, and why the presence of proofs does not make them theoretical.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge can have proofs that are implicitly known.

The objector claims that if the three necessary propositions require precise proofs, they cannot be intuitive. The commentator responds by pointing out that these proofs are implicitly observed by all rational people, even if not articulated. The meaning matters more than wording, and the proofs are present to the mind without a new act of acquisition. This lesson unpacks that response.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector argues that any proposition that depends on a proof must be theoretical, not intuitive. Therefore, if the three propositions rely on the proofs given earlier, they cannot be intuitive.
This is a common misunderstanding.
2
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage from the matn and its translation. Notice the key terms: بديهة (intuition), نظرية (theoretical), ملحوظة (observed).
The passage is short but dense.
3
Analyze the answer
The author says the proofs are 'observed' (malḥūẓa) by rational people. He gives examples: 'If the whole were not greater than the part, the other part would have no effect' etc. These are implicit proofs that people use without being aware of them as formal proofs.
4
Grasp the key principle: العبرة بالمعنى لا بالعبارة
The consideration is by meaning, not by wording. Even if people don't phrase the proof exactly as the author did, they still have the meaning in mind.
This is a fundamental principle in Islamic epistemology.
5
Conclude the intuitive status
Since the proofs are already present to the mind (without new acquisition), the propositions remain intuitive. They are not dependent on a fresh act of reasoning.
This resolves the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما أمكن أن يقال : إن كل عاقل يعلم بالبديهة حقيقة هذه القضايا الثلاث وإن لم يخطر بباله تلك الحجج الدقيقة التي أوردتموها كيف ولو توقفت عليها لكانت نظرية غير بديهية؟ أشار إلى الجواب بقوله ( وهذه الاستدلالات) التي ذكرناها ( ملحوظة ) للعقلاء ) وإن عجز البعض عن تلخيصها في التعبير عنها ألا ترى إلى قولهم لو لم يكن الكل أعظم من الجزء لم يكن للجزء الآخر أثر البتة، ولو كان الشيء الواحد مساوياً لمختلفين لكان مخالفاً لنفسه فإنه إشارة إلى ما قررناه وإن لم تكن عبارته ملخصة كما لخصناها، لكن العبرة بالمعنى لا بالعبارة وليس يلزم من توقفها على هذه الحجج كونها نظرية لجواز كون الحجج ملحوظة بلا تجشم کسب جدید
And when it might be said that every rational being knows by intuition (al-badīha) the reality of these three propositions even if they do not occur to him those precise proofs (al-ḥijaj al-daqīqa) that you have adduced, how [can that be]? For if they depended on them, they would be theoretical (naẓariyya) not intuitive? He pointed to the answer by his saying: 'And these inferences (al-istidlālāt) that we have mentioned are observed (malḥūẓa) by rational people,' even if some are unable to summarize them in expression. Do you not see to their saying: 'If the whole were not greater than the part, the other part would have no effect at all; and if a single thing were equal to two different things, it would be contrary to itself.' For it is an indication of what we have established, even if its expression is not summarized as we have summarized it. But the consideration is by meaning (al-‘ibra bi-l-ma‘nā) not by wording (al-‘ibāra). And it does not follow from their dependence on these proofs that they are theoretical, because it is possible that the proofs are observed (malḥūẓa) without undertaking new acquisition.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector argues that if the three necessary propositions (the whole is greater than the part, things equal to a common thing are equal, a body cannot be in two places simultaneously) require the precise proofs that the author has given, then they would be theoretical (naẓariyya) rather than intuitive (badīhiyya), which contradicts the claim that they are known by intuition. The author's response, quoted from the matn, is that these inferences are 'observed' (malḥūẓa) by rational people even if some cannot articulate them. He gives examples: the proof that if the whole were not greater than the part the other part would have no effect, and if one thing were equal to two different things it would be self-contradictory. These are indications of what the author has established, even if not expressed in the same precise wording. The key point is that the meaning matters, not the wording. The dependence of these propositions on proofs does not make them theoretical because the proofs can be implicitly present to the mind without a new act of acquisition. Thus the intuitive status is preserved.

Key Terms
قضايا ثلاث
بديهة
حجج
نظرية
ملحوظة
العبرة بالمعنى لا بالعبارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessary propositions cannot have any proofs; if they do, they become theoretical.
Correction: The author shows that necessary propositions can have proofs as long as those proofs are implicitly observed by the mind without a new act of acquisition.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do the three necessary propositions remain intuitive despite being supported by proofs?
A) Because the proofs are simple and everyone can articulate them.
B) Because the proofs are implicitly observed by the mind without new acquisition.
C) Because the prophets taught them directly.
D) Because they are based on sense perception.

Key Takeaway
The three necessary propositions remain intuitive because their proofs are implicitly present to the mind, and the essence matters more than the precise wording.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a belief because you couldn't articulate its proof in your own words, even though you intuitively knew it was true? What does this reveal about your reliance on expression over meaning?
?
The objector demanded explicit proofs to accept intuitive knowledge. In what area of your life do you demand perfect clarity from yourself before acting, and how does that delay your trust in what you already know?
?
When you accept a truth only when you can explain it elegantly, are you exalting your own articulation above the truth itself? How can you begin to value meaning over wording in your daily understanding of faith?

The Nature of Mawsufiyya and Lā-mawsūfiyya
Defines mawsufiyya and lā-mawsūfiyya, explaining why mawsufiyya is not 'adamiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why mawsufiyya (attribution) is considered existential (wujūdiyya) and not non-existential ('adamiyya), based on its opposition to lā-mawsūfiyya.

Core Idea
Mawsufiyya is existential because it is the opposite of non-existential lā-mawsūfiyya.

This passage establishes a key distinction in Islamic ontology: the nature of attribution (mawsufiyya) as either existential or non-existential. The author argues that mawsufiyya cannot be non-existential because it is the direct opposite of lā-mawsūfiyya (non-attribution), which is clearly non-existential. You will see how this classification is grounded in logical consistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the concise definitional style.
The passage uses parentheses to embed commentary.
2
Identify the two key terms
Focus on 'mawsufiyya' and 'lā-mawsūfiyya'. Understand that they are opposites (naqīḍ).
Opposites cannot both be non-existential.
3
Understand the proof for lā-mawsūfiyya being non-existential
The passage argues that lā-mawsūfiyya is non-existential because it applies to non-existents (e.g., a nonexistent thing is not described by colors).
4
Connect to the learning objective
Reflect: if lā-mawsūfiyya is non-existential and mawsufiyya is its opposite, then mawsufiyya must be existential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بينهما موصوفية، ولا يمكن اعتبارها علي وجه يصح ) لأن الموصوفية ليست عدمية لأنه نقيض اللاموصوفية) وتذكير الضمير للنظر إلى الخبر ) وهي ) أي اللاموصوفية عدمية لصدقها على المعدوم) فإن المعدومات لا تتصف بالألوان والحركات
قوله : ( لأن الموصوفية إلخ) لم يقل هاهنا أن الموصوفية لكونها مغايرة للموضوع تحتاج إلى موصوفية أخرى، باعتبارها تحمل ،وهكذا، فلزم التسلسل كما ذكره سابقاً لأن هذا الوجه مبني على جواز التسلسل في الأمور الاعتبارية حيث خص لزوم التسلسل على تقدير كون الموصوفية وجودية، ولأن فيه تكثير الوجوه القادحة.
قوله : (أي اللاموصوفية أي مفهومها فيكفي في ذلك صدقها على المعدوم إذ لو كانت وجودية امتنع اتصاف المعدوم بها فما قيل من أن المراد بعدمية اللاموصوفية عدمية جميع أفرادها، وهي إنما تثبت لو ثبت صدقها دائماً على المعدوم، وهم محض وكذا، ما قيل : عدمية صورة النفي موقوفة على وجودية مدخل حرف النفي، فالاستدلال بعدميتها على وجودية المدخول دور والجواب أن موقوفية عدمية صورة النفي على وجودية المدخول لا ينافي كون العلم بوجودية المدخول مستفاداً من العلم بعدميتها بوجه آخر، كما فيما نحن فيه وهكذا الحال في كل معلوم بالقياس إلى علته .
Between them is attribution (mawsufiyya), and it cannot be considered in a valid manner, because attribution is not non-existential ('adamiyya) since it is the opposite of non-attribution (al-lā-mawsūfiyya). And the masculine pronoun refers to the predicate. And it (i.e., non-attribution) is non-existential because it applies to the non-existent, for non-existents are not described by colors and movements. His statement: 'Because attribution, etc.' He did not say here that attribution, because it is different from the subject, needs another attribution, considering it as predicated, and so on, so regress would follow as he mentioned earlier, because this aspect is based on the permissibility of regress in relational matters (al-umūr al-iʿtibāriyya), since he specified the necessity of regress on the supposition that attribution is existential (wujūdiyya), and because it multiplies the objectionable aspects. His statement: 'i.e., non-attribution, i.e., its concept, so it suffices for that that it applies to the non-existent, because if it were existential, it would be impossible for the non-existent to be described by it. So what is said that what is meant by the non-existentiality of non-attribution is the non-existentiality of all its individuals, and that is only established if its application to the non-existent is constant, is pure illusion. Similarly, what is said: the non-existentiality of the form of negation is dependent on the existentiality of the object of the negation particle, so inferring its existentiality from its non-existentiality is circular. The answer: that the dependence of the non-existentiality of the form of negation on the existentiality of the object does not contradict the fact that knowledge of the existentiality of the object is derived from knowledge of its non-existentiality in another way, as in our case, and such is the case for everything known in relation to its cause.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on a previous statement about the nature of attribution (mawsufiyya). It begins by stating that there is an attribution between subject and predicate, and it cannot be considered validly. The reason given is that attribution is not non-existential ('adamiyya) because it is the opposite of non-attribution (lā-mawsūfiyya). The commentator clarifies that non-attribution is non-existential because it applies to the non-existent, whereas non-existents cannot have attributes like colors and movements. Then the commentary addresses a previous statement ('because attribution, etc.') and explains why the author did not mention the regress argument here as he did earlier. The reason is that the regress argument in this context is based on allowing regress in relational matters, and the author restricts the regress to the case where attribution is existential (wujūdiyya). The commentator then defends the definition of non-attribution as non-existential by noting that its concept suffices to apply to the non-existent; if it were existential, it could not apply to non-existents. He refutes two objections: first, that the non-existentiality of non-attribution would require constant application to the non-existent (which is denied as pure illusion); second, that the non-existentiality of the form of negation depends on the existentiality of the negated term, making the inference circular. The answer to the second objection is that the dependence does not prevent deriving existentiality from non-existentiality in another way, as in this case.

Correction: This passage corrects that by showing mawsufiyya is existential because it is the opposite of the clearly non-existential lā-mawsūfiyya.

Why does the author claim that mawsufiyya is not 'adamiyya?

Key Takeaway
Mawsufiyya is existential because it is the opposite of the non-existential concept lā-mawsūfiyya.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage argues that mawsufiyya (attribution) is existential because it is opposite to lā-mawsūfiyya, which applies to non-existent things. You are a being with many attributes—your character, knowledge, faith. Are there moments when you treat these attributes as if they lack real existence, as if they don't define you or matter for your eternal state? How does recognizing that your attributes are real and existential (since you exist) compel you to develop them with urgency before Allah?
?
The author proves lā-mawsūfiyya is non-existential because it 'applies to the non-existent.' Think about your own non-existence before birth and the fact that you could have been among the 'ma'dūmāt.' Does this realization humble your pride and make you grateful to Allah for granting you existence and existential attributes? What specific attribute will you thank Him for today?
?
If mawsufiyya were non-existential, it could not be the opposite of a non-existential concept—yet the logic demands it is existential. Now search your heart: do you ever treat your relationship with Allah (your tawḥīd, your worship) as if it were a mere mental construct, a 'non-existential' idea with no real consequences? How can you transform that relationship into an existential reality that shapes every action?

The Regress Objection and Defense of Lā-mawsūfiyya
Addresses why regress is not mentioned here and defends the 'adamiyya of lā-mawsūfiyya against two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author avoids the regress argument here and how he defends the non-existentiality of lā-mawsūfiyya against two objections.

Core Idea
Regress is avoided because it depends on mawsufiyya being existential, and the defense of lā-mawsūfiyya as non-existential is maintained.

This passage continues the discussion on mawsufiyya by addressing a potential objection: why isn't the infinite regress argument raised here as before? The author explains that the regress would only apply if mawsufiyya were existential, and since he allows regress in relational matters, he limits it. Then he defends the non-existentiality of lā-mawsūfiyya against two sophisticated objections, providing nuanced answers.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the second part of the passage carefully. It includes the author's explanation and two objections with answers.
This part is more technical; take your time.
2
Understand why regress is not raised
Note that the author deliberately avoids the regress argument here because it is based on the supposition that mawsufiyya is existential, and he allows regress in conceptual matters.
3
Analyze the first objection and refutation
The first objection claims that the non-existentiality of lā-mawsūfiyya would require constant application to the non-existent. The author dismisses this as pure illusion.
4
Analyze the second objection and answer
The second objection involves circularity: the non-existentiality of the negation form depends on the existentiality of its object. The answer explains that this dependence does not prevent deriving existentiality from non-existentiality via a different route.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بينهما موصوفية، ولا يمكن اعتبارها علي وجه يصح ) لأن الموصوفية ليست عدمية لأنه نقيض اللاموصوفية) وتذكير الضمير للنظر إلى الخبر ) وهي ) أي اللاموصوفية عدمية لصدقها على المعدوم) فإن المعدومات لا تتصف بالألوان والحركات
قوله : ( لأن الموصوفية إلخ) لم يقل هاهنا أن الموصوفية لكونها مغايرة للموضوع تحتاج إلى موصوفية أخرى، باعتبارها تحمل ،وهكذا، فلزم التسلسل كما ذكره سابقاً لأن هذا الوجه مبني على جواز التسلسل في الأمور الاعتبارية حيث خص لزوم التسلسل على تقدير كون الموصوفية وجودية، ولأن فيه تكثير الوجوه القادحة.
قوله : (أي اللاموصوفية أي مفهومها فيكفي في ذلك صدقها على المعدوم إذ لو كانت وجودية امتنع اتصاف المعدوم بها فما قيل من أن المراد بعدمية اللاموصوفية عدمية جميع أفرادها، وهي إنما تثبت لو ثبت صدقها دائماً على المعدوم، وهم محض وكذا، ما قيل : عدمية صورة النفي موقوفة على وجودية مدخل حرف النفي، فالاستدلال بعدميتها على وجودية المدخول دور والجواب أن موقوفية عدمية صورة النفي على وجودية المدخول لا ينافي كون العلم بوجودية المدخول مستفاداً من العلم بعدميتها بوجه آخر، كما فيما نحن فيه وهكذا الحال في كل معلوم بالقياس إلى علته .
Between them is attribution (mawsufiyya), and it cannot be considered in a valid manner, because attribution is not non-existential ('adamiyya) since it is the opposite of non-attribution (al-lā-mawsūfiyya). And the masculine pronoun refers to the predicate. And it (i.e., non-attribution) is non-existential because it applies to the non-existent, for non-existents are not described by colors and movements. His statement: 'Because attribution, etc.' He did not say here that attribution, because it is different from the subject, needs another attribution, considering it as predicated, and so on, so regress would follow as he mentioned earlier, because this aspect is based on the permissibility of regress in relational matters (al-umūr al-iʿtibāriyya), since he specified the necessity of regress on the supposition that attribution is existential (wujūdiyya), and because it multiplies the objectionable aspects. His statement: 'i.e., non-attribution, i.e., its concept, so it suffices for that that it applies to the non-existent, because if it were existential, it would be impossible for the non-existent to be described by it. So what is said that what is meant by the non-existentiality of non-attribution is the non-existentiality of all its individuals, and that is only established if its application to the non-existent is constant, is pure illusion. Similarly, what is said: the non-existentiality of the form of negation is dependent on the existentiality of the object of the negation particle, so inferring its existentiality from its non-existentiality is circular. The answer: that the dependence of the non-existentiality of the form of negation on the existentiality of the object does not contradict the fact that knowledge of the existentiality of the object is derived from knowledge of its non-existentiality in another way, as in our case, and such is the case for everything known in relation to its cause.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on a previous statement about the nature of attribution (mawsufiyya). It begins by stating that there is an attribution between subject and predicate, and it cannot be considered validly. The reason given is that attribution is not non-existential ('adamiyya) because it is the opposite of non-attribution (lā-mawsūfiyya). The commentator clarifies that non-attribution is non-existential because it applies to the non-existent, whereas non-existents cannot have attributes like colors and movements. Then the commentary addresses a previous statement ('because attribution, etc.') and explains why the author did not mention the regress argument here as he did earlier. The reason is that the regress argument in this context is based on allowing regress in relational matters, and the author restricts the regress to the case where attribution is existential (wujūdiyya). The commentator then defends the definition of non-attribution as non-existential by noting that its concept suffices to apply to the non-existent; if it were existential, it could not apply to non-existents. He refutes two objections: first, that the non-existentiality of non-attribution would require constant application to the non-existent (which is denied as pure illusion); second, that the non-existentiality of the form of negation depends on the existentiality of the negated term, making the inference circular. The answer to the second objection is that the dependence does not prevent deriving existentiality from non-existentiality in another way, as in this case.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author avoided a regress argument by carefully distinguishing between existential and non-existential concepts. Is there a belief or assumption in your own life—about God, yourself, or others—that you have been defending with arguments that might collapse if you examined their underlying distinctions? Will you pause today to re-examine that assumption honestly before God?
?
The second objection claims that dependence implies circularity, but the author shows that knowledge can come from a different route. When you find yourself trapped in what seems like a logical contradiction in your faith, do you immediately assume it must be a genuine contradiction, or do you allow for the possibility that your epistemic path might be incomplete? What one contradiction are you willing to revisit with this humility?
?
The refutation of the constant-application objection is dismissed as pure illusion (wahm mahḍ). Is there an argument you have been making that, when scrutinized, turns out to be built on a false assumption you never questioned? Ask Allah to grant you the courage to label it as 'pure illusion' and abandon it.

Al-Abharī's Objection: Does Predication Imply Attribution?
Al-Abharī's objection and initial reply about predication and attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Abharī's objection that predication does not necessarily entail mawsufiyya, and the initial response denying the validity of the counterexample.

Core Idea
Predication does not automatically create a relation of attribution: a critical challenge.

Al-Abharī introduces a sharp objection: if every predication implied an attribution (mawsufiyya), then ordinary chains like 'the animal is a body, man is an animal' would lead to an infinite regress. This forces defenders of the original claim to clarify its scope. The initial response simply rejects the counterexample as invalid, but this lesson examines both the objection and the first line of defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage (objection and answer)
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the structure: an objector speaks, then an answer is given.
The passage begins with a reference to the previous argument ('his statement: for he considered...').
2
Identify the core objection
The objector says: 'We do not accept that predication here necessitates attribution.' Find the reason given: otherwise, the chain 'animal is a body, man is an animal' would invalidate it.
This is a classic infinite regress argument.
3
Understand the initial answer
The answer claims the counterexample is 'general and not sufficient' because the original reasoner can deny the validity of that form. Reflect on what this means: the objector's chain may not be a proper counterexample.
The answer is brief and leaves room for further debate.
4
Evaluate the strength of the objection and answer
Consider why this objection matters for the doctrine of attributes. Is the answer satisfactory? This sets up the rest of the discussion.
This prepares for the more detailed issues that follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فقد اعتبر بينهما موصوفية إلخ) قال الأبهري : لقائل أن يقول : لا نسلم أن الحمل يقضي هاهنا الموصوفية، وإلا انتقض بقولنا الحيوان جسم، والإنسان حيوان، إلى ما لا والجواب أن ما ذكره نقض إجمالي لا يشفى لأن المعلل يمنع صحة صورة النقض كما لا يخفى فإن قلت : الحاكم بمغايرة مفهوم الأسود للجسم حاكم بمغايرة مفهوم الموصوف له، فيحتاج إلى اعتبار موصوفية أخرى، ويتسلسل فلم لم يتعرض له ؟ قلت : لما سبق الإشارة إلى هذا المحذور لم يتعرض له ،هاهنا وأشار إلى محذور آخر على أن تعيين المغايرة في المثال المذكور باعتبار أن الغرض فيه أن يكون الترديد بين ثبوت الشيء لغيره وسلبه عنه لا بين ثبوته وانتفائه في نفسه، فلهذا لم يتعرض لاحتمال العينية، رغير متحقق بالنظر إلى الموصوفية، فالوجه حينئذ هو الترديد بين الينية والغيرية وقد ساق إلهيا الكلام وأما جواب الأبهري عما لزم شق الغيرية بأنا لا نسلم أن الموصوفية إذا كانت مغايرة لأحد المنتسبين يكون بينهما موصوفية أخرى، ويتسلسل وإنما يلزم ذلك أن لو كانت محمولة عليه وهو ممنوع ، فظاهر الاندفاع لأن المراد مغايرة مفهوم الموصوفية الذي اعتبر محمولاً في المثال فلا شك أنه إذا كان مغايراً للموضوع كان معنى قولنا الجسم موصوف بالسواد، أن الجسم موصوف بالموصوف بالسواد والكلام في الموصوف الثاني كالكلام في الأول وهلم جرا وتسلسل قطعاً.
His statement: 'For he considered between them an attribution, etc.' Al-Abharī said: An objector may say: We do not accept that predication (al-ḥaml) here necessitates attribution (al-mawṣūfiyya). Otherwise, it would be invalidated by our statement 'the animal is a body, man is an animal' ad infinitum. The answer: What he mentioned is a general objection that does not suffice, because the reasoner denies the validity of the form of the counterexample, as is not hidden. So if you say: The one who judges the difference between the concept of black (al-aswad) and the body judges the difference between the concept of the attributed (al-mawṣūf) to it, so he needs to consider another attribution, and it regresses; why did he not address that? I say: Because the reference to this problem was made earlier, he did not address it here, and he pointed to another problem. And the specification of difference (al-mughāyara) in the mentioned example is based on the fact that the purpose in it is to alternate between the affirmation of something for another and its negation from it, not between its affirmation and negation in itself. Therefore he did not address the possibility of identity (al-ʿayniyya), and that is not realized with regard to attribution. So the aspect then is the alternation between identity and otherness, and he pursued the discussion accordingly. As for al-Abharī's response to what is necessary from the division of otherness — that we do not accept that if attribution is different from one of the two relata, there would be another attribution between them, and it would regress; rather that would be necessary only if it were predicated of it, which is denied — it is obviously refuted because what is meant is the difference of the concept of attribution that was considered as predicate in the example. There is no doubt that if it is different from the subject, then the meaning of our statement 'the body is described (mawṣūf) by blackness' is that the body is described by the described by blackness, and the discussion about the second described is like the discussion about the first, and so on, and regress necessarily follows.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by al-Abharī and its response. Al-Abharī anticipates an objector who denies that predication necessarily implies an attribution (mawsufiyya) between subject and predicate. The objector argues that if it did, then chains like 'the animal is a body, man is an animal' would lead to infinite regress. The answer is that this is a general objection without force because the original reasoner can deny the validity of the counterexample. The commentor then addresses a further question: why did the author not consider the regress that would arise if the concept of black is considered different from the body, requiring another attribution? The reply is that the author had already signaled that problem earlier and here points to another issue. The purpose of the example is to alternate between affirmation and negation of a property for another, not between existence and non-existence of the thing itself. Therefore, the author does not consider the possibility of identity (that the attribute is the same as the subject) because that is not relevant to attribution. Instead, the alternation is between identity and otherness. The commentator then examines al-Abharī's own response to the regress problem: al-Abharī argues that if attribution is different from one of the relata, it does not automatically create another attribution between them; that would only happen if the first attribution were predicated of them, which is denied. The commentator rejects this as obviously insufficient, because the intended meaning is that the concept of attribution considered as predicate in the example is different from the subject. Consequently, saying 'the body is described by blackness' means 'the body is described by the described by blackness', leading to an infinite regress of described entities.

Key Terms
موصوفية
حمل
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any chain of predications automatically proves the necessity of attribution regress.
Correction: The objection only works if the original claim is taken universally; the defender can deny the specific form of the chain as a counterexample.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Al-Abharī's objection in this passage?
A) Predication always implies identity between subject and predicate.
B) Predication does not necessarily imply an attribution; otherwise, infinite chains would result.
C) The chain 'animal is a body, man is an animal' is impossible.
D) The concept of black is identical to the body.

Key Takeaway
Al-Abharī's objection forces a precise definition of when and why predication entails mawsufiyya, and the initial answer is too vague.

The Regress of Difference: A Further Question and Answer
Answer to the regress of difference: redefinition of the example's purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a regress arises from the difference between concepts in attribution, and how the author responds by shifting the focus to alternation between affirmation and negation.

Core Idea
If attribution is different from its subject, another attribution is needed, leading to regress — unless the purpose is alternation.

A new question arises: if the concept of black is judged different from the body, then that judgment itself requires an attribution, leading to a regress. The answer invokes an earlier signal and reinterprets the example's purpose as alternation between affirmation and negation, not between existence and non-existence. This lesson unpacks that subtle response.

Guided Path
1
Read the question about regress of difference
Find the question: 'The one who judges the difference between black and the body judges the difference between the concept of the attributed to it, so he needs another attribution, and it regresses.' Understand the structure.
This is a regress that the author had earlier signaled.
2
Understand the author's reply
The reply says the author already pointed to this problem earlier, and here he points to another problem. Then it redefines the purpose of the example: to alternate between affirmation/negation for another, not for itself.
This shifts the ground: the example is not about identity or difference of concepts per se.
3
Grasp the alternation point
The purpose is to consider whether the attribute (like black) is affirmed of the subject or negated from it, not whether it exists in itself. Therefore, the possibility of identity (ʿayniyya) is irrelevant, and the focus is on identity versus otherness.
This avoids the regress because the alternation does not require an infinite chain of attributions.
4
Connect to the overall argument
This response shows how the author defends the original claim by reinterpreting the example's logical role. It also sets up the next critique of al-Abharī's own answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The reply is that the author had already signaled that problem earlier and here points to another issue. The purpose of the example is to alternate between affirmation and negation of a property for another, not between existence and non-existence of the thing itself. Therefore, the author does not consider the possibility of identity (that the attribute is the same as the subject) because that is not relevant to attribution. Instead, the alternation is between identity and otherness. The commentator then examines al-Abharī's own response to the regress problem: al-Abharī argues that if attribution is different from one of the relata, it does not automatically create another attribution between them; that would only happen if the first attribution were predicated of them, which is denied. The commentator rejects this as obviously insufficient, because the intended meaning is that the concept of attribution considered as predicate in the example is different from the subject. Consequently, saying 'the body is described by blackness' means 'the body is described by the described by blackness', leading to an infinite regress of described entities.

Key Terms

تسلسل
عينية
غيرية
موصوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any judgment of difference between concepts leads to an infinite regress of attributions.
Correction: The regress is avoided because the example's purpose is specifically to alternate between affirmation and negation for another, not to analyze the concepts themselves.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say he did not address the regress of difference in this passage?
A) Because it is impossible.
B) Because he had already referred to it earlier.
C) Because the concept of black is identical to the body.
D) Because the alternation is between existence and non-existence.

Key Takeaway
The regress of difference is sidestepped by reinterpreting the example's purpose as alternation between affirmation and negation for another, not analysis of concepts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself trapped in endless analysis of your own characteristics, trying to find a 'true self' behind your attributes? How might the lesson's redefinition of purpose help you break free from such regress and focus on your relationship with Allah?
?
The passage distinguishes between affirming something for another vs. in itself. In your daily life, are your actions and qualities primarily for yourself or for the sake of Allah? Examine one motive today to see which category it falls into.

Refuting Al-Abharī's Own Response to the Regress
Refutation of al-Abharī's own response to the regress of attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator rejects al-Abharī's own answer to the regress problem, showing that the regress is inevitable.

Core Idea
Al-Abharī's attempt to stop the regress fails because the difference in concept itself creates a new attribution.

Al-Abharī offers his own solution: he denies that if attribution is different from a relatum, it automatically creates another attribution. The commentator immediately dismisses this as obviously inadequate, because the very concept of 'attribution' as used in the example is different from the subject, leading to an infinite chain of 'described by the described' — a clear regress. This lesson reveals the commentator's critique.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Abharī's proposed response
Al-Abharī says: 'We do not accept that if attribution is different from one of the two relata, there would be another attribution between them; that would only happen if the first attribution were predicated of them, which is denied.'
He tries to block the regress by requiring the first attribution to be predicated again.
2
Understand the commentator's refutation
The commentator says this is 'obviously refuted' because the concept of attribution that is considered as predicate in the example is indeed different from the subject. So the meaning of 'the body is described by blackness' becomes 'the body is described by the described by blackness'.
This creates a new 'described' entity at each step, leading to regress.
3
Recognize the infinite chain
The commentator explicitly states: 'the discussion about the second described is like the discussion about the first, and so on, and regress necessarily follows.'
This shows that al-Abharī's defense fails to stop the regress.
4
Reflect on the significance
This critique underscores the difficulty of avoiding regress in the metaphysics of attributes. It also shows the interplay between objector, proponent, and commentator in kalam literature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فقد اعتبر بينهما موصوفية إلخ) قال الأبهري : لقائل أن يقول : لا نسلم أن الحمل يقضي هاهنا الموصوفية، وإلا انتقض بقولنا الحيوان جسم، والإنسان حيوان، إلى ما لا والجواب أن ما ذكره نقض إجمالي لا يشفى لأن المعلل يمنع صحة صورة النقض كما لا يخفى فإن قلت : الحاكم بمغايرة مفهوم الأسود للجسم حاكم بمغايرة مفهوم الموصوف له، فيحتاج إلى اعتبار موصوفية أخرى، ويتسلسل فلم لم يتعرض له ؟ قلت : لما سبق الإشارة إلى هذا المحذور لم يتعرض له ،هاهنا وأشار إلى محذور آخر على أن تعيين المغايرة في المثال المذكور باعتبار أن الغرض فيه أن يكون الترديد بين ثبوت الشيء لغيره وسلبه عنه لا بين ثبوته وانتفائه في نفسه، فلهذا لم يتعرض لاحتمال العينية، رغير متحقق بالنظر إلى الموصوفية، فالوجه حينئذ هو الترديد بين الينية والغيرية وقد ساق إلهيا الكلام وأما جواب الأبهري عما لزم شق الغيرية بأنا لا نسلم أن الموصوفية إذا كانت مغايرة لأحد المنتسبين يكون بينهما موصوفية أخرى، ويتسلسل وإنما يلزم ذلك أن لو كانت محمولة عليه وهو ممنوع ، فظاهر الاندفاع لأن المراد مغايرة مفهوم الموصوفية الذي اعتبر محمولاً في المثال فلا شك أنه إذا كان مغايراً للموضوع كان معنى قولنا الجسم موصوف بالسواد، أن الجسم موصوف بالموصوف بالسواد والكلام في الموصوف الثاني كالكلام في الأول وهلم جرا وتسلسل قطعاً.
His statement: 'For he considered between them an attribution, etc.' Al-Abharī said: An objector may say: We do not accept that predication (al-ḥaml) here necessitates attribution (al-mawṣūfiyya). Otherwise, it would be invalidated by our statement 'the animal is a body, man is an animal' ad infinitum. The answer: What he mentioned is a general objection that does not suffice, because the reasoner denies the validity of the form of the counterexample, as is not hidden. So if you say: The one who judges the difference between the concept of black (al-aswad) and the body judges the difference between the concept of the attributed (al-mawṣūf) to it, so he needs to consider another attribution, and it regresses; why did he not address that? I say: Because the reference to this problem was made earlier, he did not address it here, and he pointed to another problem. And the specification of difference (al-mughāyara) in the mentioned example is based on the fact that the purpose in it is to alternate between the affirmation of something for another and its negation from it, not between its affirmation and negation in itself. Therefore he did not address the possibility of identity (al-ʿayniyya), and that is not realized with regard to attribution. So the aspect then is the alternation between identity and otherness, and he pursued the discussion accordingly. As for al-Abharī's response to what is necessary from the division of otherness — that we do not accept that if attribution is different from one of the two relata, there would be another attribution between them, and it would regress; rather that would be necessary only if it were predicated of it, which is denied — it is obviously refuted because what is meant is the difference of the concept of attribution that was considered as predicate in the example. There is no doubt that if it is different from the subject, then the meaning of our statement 'the body is described (mawṣūf) by blackness' is that the body is described by the described by blackness, and the discussion about the second described is like the discussion about the first, and so on, and regress necessarily follows.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by al-Abharī and its response. Al-Abharī anticipates an objector who denies that predication necessarily implies an attribution (mawsufiyya) between subject and predicate. The objector argues that if it did, then chains like 'the animal is a body, man is an animal' would lead to infinite regress. The answer is that this is a general objection without force because the original reasoner can deny the validity of the counterexample. The commentor then addresses a further question: why did the author not consider the regress that would arise if the concept of black is considered different from the body, requiring another attribution? The reply is that the author had already signaled that problem earlier and here points to another issue. The purpose of the example is to alternate between affirmation and negation of a property for another, not between existence and non-existence of the thing itself. Therefore, the author does not consider the possibility of identity (that the attribute is the same as the subject) because that is not relevant to attribution. Instead, the alternation is between identity and otherness. The commentator then examines al-Abharī's own response to the regress problem: al-Abharī argues that if attribution is different from one of the relata, it does not automatically create another attribution between them; that would only happen if the first attribution were predicated of them, which is denied. The commentator rejects this as obviously insufficient, because the intended meaning is that the concept of attribution considered as predicate in the example is different from the subject. Consequently, saying 'the body is described by blackness' means 'the body is described by the described by blackness', leading to an infinite regress of described entities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a regress of attributions can be avoided if the first attribution is not itself predicated.
Correction: The commentator shows that the concept of attribution in the example is precisely predicated, so regress is unavoidable.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator reject al-Abharī's response to the regress?
A) Because the regress is impossible by definition.
B) Because the concept of attribution in the example is different from the subject, making the regress inevitable.
C) Because the body cannot be described by blackness.
D) Because al-Abharī does not use the term 'mawsufiyya' correctly.

Key Takeaway
Al-Abharī's own response fails because the concept of attribution itself acts as a predicate, generating an infinite chain of description.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you present a counterargument to a truth you know, do you examine whether your own assumptions are as trivially refutable as al-Abharī's? Will you accept correction even when it exposes the weakness of your reasoning, or will you persist in defending a flawed position?
?
Consider an area in your life where you impose artificial conditions to avoid confronting an uncomfortable logical consequence—like al-Abharī denying that attribution is predicated. Are you willing to admit that the very thing you tried to exclude is already present, and let the truth reshape your understanding?
?
The commentator calls al-Abharī's response 'obviously refuted'. Is there a belief you hold that, if honestly examined, would face a similar obvious refutation? What would it cost you to lay down that defense and embrace the full weight of the argument?

Objections and Clarifications on the Nature of Attribution
Objections and responses on mawsufiyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends the proof of attribution (mawsufiyya) against objections about conceivability, existential status, and regress.

Core Idea
Attribution can be conceived without essential knowledge and is not undermined by circularity or regress.

The proof of mawsufiyya established that subject and attribute require an attribution. But objections arise: can we conceive attribution without knowing essences? Is attribution existential or non-existential? And does it lead to regress? This passage walks through these objections and their resolutions. Follow closely to see how the commentator clarifies each point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage along with the English translation. Do not worry about full understanding yet; just get a sense of the flow of objections and responses.
The passage is dense with dialectical exchanges.
2
Identify the objections
Look for the key objections: (1) that conceivability requires essential knowledge, (2) that the existential status of attribution leads to contradiction, (3) that inferring existentiality from non-existentiality is circular, (4) that affirmationality and non-existentiality cannot coexist, and (5) that regress occurs even under non-existential attribution.
3
Trace the commentator's responses
For each objection, note the commentator's reply: he denies the premise, clarifies the meaning, invokes a distinction (e.g., between nature and individuals), or shifts the burden of proof. See how each defense reinforces the original proof.
The response to circularity is particularly nuanced.
4
Reflect on the key concepts
Focus on the terms 'thubūtiyya' (affirmationality) and 'wujūdiyya' (existentiality). Understand why the commentator insists that the attribution is neither merely non-existential nor merely existential, but something that can be conceived without contradiction.
The commentator says this is the beginning of the problem, not a concession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فلا يعقلان (دونها أي لا يعقلان متجاوزين عنها بأن لا يكون بينهما موصوفية وهو ظاهر البطلان، لأنا نعقل كل واحد من الجسم والسواد بدون الآخر، فينفكان عن الوصفية فما قيل : إنما يظهر البطلان إذا ثبت تعقل شيء من الموصوفات والصفات بالكنه، وهو ممنوع ناشئ من سوء فهم العبارة.
قوله : (موصوفية بها أي موصوفية موجودة بتلك الموصوفية الوجودية لما مر . قوله : ( وإذا لم تكن إلخ) وأيضاً يلزم اجتماع الوجود والعدم في قولكم الشيء، إما أن يكون أو لا يكون .
بها معدومة، وليس المراد أن تلك الطبيعة نفسها عدمية في الجملة حتى يثبت بعدمية فرد من موصوفها، وأيضاً عدمية صورة النفي مبنية على وجودية مدخول حرف النفي، فالاستدلال على وجوديته بعدميتها ،دور، اللهم إلا أن يمنع عدم كفاية عدمية الطبيعة نفسها في الاستدلال على عدم إمكان اعتبار الموصوفية بين الموضوع والمحمول، اعتباراً صحيحاً . حتى يثبت أن لا يكون للجزء الثبوتي معنى صحيح، وعدم كفاية جزئية حرف السلب في استلزام عدمية الطبيعة في الجملة حتى يلزم الدور فتأمل.
قوله : ( ولا وجودية) فإن قلت : لا يجتمع ثبوتية الموصوفية وعدم وجوديتها، قلت : لا نسلم بل هو هاهنا أول المسألة.
قوله : ( فلا يعقلان دونها وهو ظاهر (البطلان إنما يظهر البطلان إذا ثبت تعقل شيء من الموصوفات الصفات بالكنه، وثبوت تعقل شيء من الماهيات بالكنه ممنوع. :قوله : ( فلهما حينئذ موصوفية بها فيتسلسل فإن قلت : اتصافهما بالموصوفية هاهنا ثابت على تقدير عدميتها أيضاً، إذ لا شك في عدم كونها حينئذ نفس الموصوف والصفة، ولا اتصاف الموجودات بالعدميات في نفس الأمر، فلم لم يتعرض له على تقدير عدميتها ؟ قلت : لأنه قد لا نسلم حينئذ بطلان التسلسل كما أشار إليه المصنف فيما سبق فتأمل.
His statement: 'So they are not conceivable without it' i.e., they are not conceivable as transcending it such that there is no attribution between them, and this is obviously false, because we conceive each one of the body and blackness without the other, so they are separable from attribution. So what is said: 'Impossibility is only apparent if the conception of something of the described and attributes by essence (bi-l-kunh) is established, which is denied, arising from a misunderstanding of the expression.' His statement: 'Attribution by it' i.e., an existing attribution by that existential attribution, as passed. His statement: 'And if it is not, etc.' And also the conjunction of existence and non-existence would be necessary in your statement: 'A thing is either existent or non-existent.' ...by it is non-existent, and it is not meant that that nature itself is non-existential absolutely so that it is proven by the non-existence of an individual of its described. Also, the non-existentiality of the form of negation is based on the existentiality of the object of the negation particle, so inferring its existentiality from its non-existentiality is circular, unless one denies the insufficiency of the non-existentiality of the nature itself in inferring the impossibility of considering attribution between subject and predicate validly, until it is proven that there is no correct meaning for the affirmational part, and the insufficiency of the particle of negation in necessitating the non-existentiality of the nature absolutely such that circularity would follow, so reflect. His statement: 'And not existential': if you say: the affirmationality (thubūtiyya) of attribution and its non-existentiality cannot coexist, I say: we do not accept; rather here it is the beginning of the issue. His statement: 'So they are not conceivable without it, and it is obviously false' – impossibility is only apparent if the conception of something of the described and attributes by essence is established, but the establishment of conception of something of essences (māhiyyāt) by essence is denied. His statement: 'So for them then an attribution by it, so it regresses': if you say: their being described by attribution here is established upon the supposition of its non-existentiality as well, for there is no doubt that it is then not the subject and attribute themselves, and existents are not described by non-existentials in reality. So why did he not address this upon the supposition of its non-existentiality? I say: because then we may not accept the impossibility of regress, as the author indicated previously, so reflect.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage contains several successive objections and clarifications regarding the proof of attribution. The first objection is about the statement 'they are not conceivable without it'. The commentator clarifies that this does not mean they cannot be conceived at all without attribution; rather, we can conceive body and blackness separately, so they are separable from attribution. The objection that this only holds if we conceive essences by their inner reality (kunh) is dismissed as a misunderstanding. The next part explains the phrase 'attribution by it' (mawṣūfiyya bi-hā) as an existential attribution derived from the earlier argument. Then an objection is raised that if attribution is neither non-existential nor existential, then the universal division 'a thing is either existent or non-existent' would imply a contradiction. The commentator then addresses a circularity objection: using the non-existentiality of the negation to prove the existentiality of the negated term is circular, but the response is that the non-existentiality of the nature itself is sufficient to show the impossibility of a valid attribution, even if the negation particle's form depends on existence. He then takes up the statement 'and not existential': an objector says that affirmationality (thubūtiyya) and non-existentiality cannot coexist; the commentator replies that this is not conceded; rather this is the beginning of the problem. Finally, he addresses a point about regress: if attribution is supposed to be non-existential, then subject and attribute are not described by it in reality, so regress might not be problematic; the commentator says that in that case we may not accept the impossibility of regress, as the author hinted earlier.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument's impossibility relies on conceiving essences by their inner reality (kunh).
Correction: The argument only requires the general conceivability of body and blackness separately, which does not entail essential knowledge.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator respond to the objection that the argument requires conceiving essences by their inner reality?
A) He agrees and modifies the argument.
B) He denies it, stating that we can conceive body and blackness without each other, so they are separable from attribution.
C) He says the objection is irrelevant because attribution is existential.
D) He claims that essences are unknowable.

Key Takeaway
Attribution can be conceived without essential knowledge, and the commentator defends the proof against objections about existential status and regress without conceding any essential point.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, the commentator denies that essential knowledge of the subject and attribute is required for attribution. How often do you demand to understand the 'essence' of Allah (e.g., His nature) before you feel secure in affirming His attributes? Are you willing to accept that you can know Him truly without grasping His innermost reality? Make a commitment today to focus on affirming His attributes with humility, not demanding to comprehend His essence.
?
When faced with objections to your beliefs—especially logical objections about circularity or regress—are you quick to dismiss them like the objectors here, or do you carefully consider whether your understanding is based on a misunderstanding? This lesson shows that even dense logical puzzles can be resolved without conceding the truth. What intellectual objection have you been avoiding? Resolve to study it with the same rigor shown here, trusting that truth withstands scrutiny.

The Two Aspects of the Non-Existence of Absolute Non-Existence
Commentary on the dual nature of the non-existence of absolute non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the non-existence of absolute non-existence can be both a part (qism) and an opposite (qasīm) of absolute non-existence, depending on the perspective.

Core Idea
One thing can be both part and opposite by different considerations.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif introduces a key conceptual distinction: the entity 'non-existence of absolute non-existence' can be viewed in two ways. As a raising of absolute non-existence, it is its opposite; as a specific non-existence, it is a part of it. Understanding this duality is essential for later discussions on predication and the nature of non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before interpreting.
Pay attention to the terms 'رفع' and 'عدم خاص'.
2
Identify the two considerations
Locate the phrases 'من حيث أنه رفع للعدم المطلق' and 'من حيث أنه عدم خاص'. These indicate the two perspectives that make the same thing both a part and an opposite.
3
Understand the four objections
The commentator addresses four possible objections. Read each objection and its refutation, noticing how they hinge on the distinction between absolute non-existence and specific non-existence.
This shows the practical importance of the dual consideration.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that the perspective (اعتبار) determines whether something is a type (نوع) or an opposite. Ask yourself: why does this matter for proving necessary sciences?
Connects to the broader goal of establishing rational foundations.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الموجود على السواد إنما صح ( للتغاير مفهوماً فإن مفهوم السواد مغاير لمفهوم الموجود والاتحاد (هوية) أي ذاتاً صدقاً عليه فلا يلزم هاهنا عدم الإفادة، كما في قولنا : أنه ليس بموجود أيضاً، ولا يلزم ثبوت الواسطة لأن العدم لا يقبل هذه القسمة، وليس بشيء أما أولاً فلان العبارة لا تساعده، إذ اللائق حينئذ وليس في ذلك كون قسيم الشيء قسماً منه، وأما ثانياً فلان الكلام في عدم العدم المطلق، وأنه قسم من العدم
