المطلق وهو عدم خاص. قوله : (إذ يكون عدم العدم المطلق إلخ) يعني أن هذا المقيد من حيث إنه عدم مقيد مع قطع النظر عن خصوصية القيد نوع منه ومن حيث إنه رفع للعدم مقابل له، فالمنظور في الاعتبار الأول كونه عدماً مقيداً بقيد وحينئذ الاعتبار الثاني هو كونه رفع العدم وسلبه، فالموضع مختلف باعتبار كذا أفاده بعض المحققين. قوله ( والاتحاد هوبة) قال المصنف في بحث الماهية ومعنى حمل الحيوان على الإنسان هذين المفهومين المتغايرين في العقل هويتهما الخارجية والوهمية واحدة، فلا يلزم وحدة الاثنين ولا حمل الشيء على نفسه، وقال الشارح: إن التفسير المذكور لا يطرد في نحو الإنسان أعمى، إذ لا هوية لمفهوم الأعمى متحدة مع هوية الإنسان وإلا لكان موجوداً خارجياً، فلهذا صرف المتن عن ظاهره وفسره بما هو المختار عنده أي الاتحاد هوية باعتبار الصدق لا أن المعدوم المطلق المراد به المعدوم في الذهن والخارج، إذ العدم موجود في الذهن ولأن العدم ليس بمعدوم، وإلا لزم ثبوت الشيء لنفسه كما أنه ليس بموجود أيضاً، ولا يلزم ثبوت الواسطة لأن العدم لا يقبل هذه القسمة كما أشير إليه في التجريد معين قوله : ( من حيث إنه رفع للعدم المطلق أراد بالعدم المطلق العدم الغير المضاف إلى شيء لا العدم في الذهن والخارج أي عدم الوجود الذهني والخارجي، كما أن المراد بالعدم المطلق المعدوم فيهما وإلا لم يصح كون العدم الخاص قسما منه، إذ لا يصدق عليه أنه عدم الوجود المطلق، بل هو عدم العدم فإن قلت: قسم الشيء مثبت له لا رافع، وأيضاً رفع العدم وجود وهو لا يكون قسماً من العدم بالبداهة قلت القسمية الإثبات بحسب الذات، والقسيمية الرفع بحسب المفهوم، ثم رفع العدم مستلزم للوجود لا نفسه وإن أشعر به كلام الشارح في بحث التقابل والاستلزام لا يقدح في القسمية . المرصد الرابع - في إثبات العلوم الضرورية السواد سواد ولا الحكم بوحدة الاثنين، فهذا جواب عن الدليل الثاني في الشق الأول الذي هو طرف الثبوت من الترديد الأول من الوجه الثالث أعني قوله : وأيضاً فإنه حكم بوحدة الاثنين وترك جواب الدليل الأول في هذا الشق أعني قوله: فهو في نفسه معدوم إلخ اعتمادا على ما سيجيء من أن الماهية في حد ذاتها ليست موجودة ولا معدومة هويته عين هويته، لكن قال المحقق الدواني ناقلاً عن الشيخ : إن الأمور العدمية المحمولة على الشيء متحدة معه بالعرض لكونها منتزعة منه وإن لم تكن متحدة معه حقيقة فتفسير الحمل بالاتحاد بالهوية جار في الذاتيات والعرضيات والأمور العدمية أقول ولعل هذا هو المراد بالاتحاد في الصدق فمرجع التفسيرين واحد. قوله : أي ذاتاً صدقاً عليه فإن قلت الصدق الموصول بعلى معناه الحمل، فيلزم أخذ الحمل في تفسيره قلت : هذا بيان لوجه صحته، وأما تفسيره فهو الحكم بالاتحاد بين الشيئين، وبهذا ظهر أن تفسيره بالتغاير في المفهوم الاتحاد في الصدق كما اختار الشارح فيما سيأتي غير صحيح. قوله : (فهذا جواب عن الدليل أراد بالشق الأول أن يكون المتردد فيه ثبوت الشيء وعدمه في نفسه وقوله : أعني قوله: وأيضاً إلخ بيان للدليل الثاني، وقد عرفت فيما سبق أن الترديد المذكور بقوله : إما نفسه أو غيره بحسب الذات والمفهوم فحاصل الجواب : أن لا نسلم لزوم الحكم بوحدة الاثنين على تقدير المغايرة، لأن المحال إنما هو الحكم بوحدة الاثنين من حيث إنهما اثنان وهاهنا ليس كذلك لأن التغاير من حيث المفهوم، والاتحاد من حيث الهوية، وبهذا ظهر أنه لا يتم الجواب بدون بيان جهتي التغاير والاتحاد . قوله : ( من أن الماهية في حد ذاتها إلخ) بناء على أن شيئاً منهما ليس نفسها ولا داخلاً فيها، فهما مسلوبان عنها في مرتبتها وارتفاع النقيضين في المرتبة جائز، وإذا لم تكن في نفسها معدومة لم يلزم من قيام الوجود بها قيام الموجود بالمعدوم . قوله : ( ولا الحكم بوحدة الاثنين) أي الاتحاد الفاسد وهو اتحاد الاثنين ذاتاً، وأما اتحاد الاثنين اللذين هما المفهومان المتغايران بحسب الذات فلا محذور فيه . قوله : (فهذا جواب عن الدليل الثاني في الشق الأول أراد بالشق الأول أن يكون المراد فيه ثبوت الشيء وعدمه في نفسه، ثم أن كون ما ذكر جواباً عما ذكر، إنما هو إذا لم يكن مراد المستدل بالنفسية والغيرية هما بحسب الخارج، إذ لو أريد ذلك لكان جواباً عن إبطال النفسية، ويكون التقدير والحمل، إنما أفاد التغاير مفهوماً، لكن قوله : والاتحاد هوية لا يخلو عن شائبة اللغوية حينئذ إلا أن يحمل على دفع وهم، فالأظهر أن يراد النفسية بحسب الذات والمفهوم ویراد بغيريتهما الغيرية بحسب مجموعهما لا بحسب كل منهما فتدبر. قوله : ( على ما سيجيء من أن الماهية في حد ذاتها ليست موجودة ولا معدومة قيل : عليه معنى هذا الكلام أن أحدهما ليس عينها ولا داخلاً فيها، لا أنها في نفسها منفكة عن أحدهما،
The Fourth Observatory - On Proving Necessary Sciences. Concerning the raising of the reality of non-existence, and there is no impossibility in it either, for the non-existence of absolute non-existence, inasmuch as it is a raising of absolute non-existence, is its opposite (qasīm), and inasmuch as it is a specific non-existence, it is a part (qism) of it. (And predication) i.e., the predication of existent upon blackness is only correct (due to conceptual distinction) for the concept of blackness is different from the concept of existent, and the union (al-ittiḥād) is in identity (huwiyya) i.e., essence (dhāt) in truth (ṣidqan) upon it, so there is no lack of benefit here, as in our statement: 'It is not existent also,' and the establishment of a middle term does not follow because non-existence does not admit this division, and it is not a thing. Firstly, because the expression does not support it, for what is fitting then is not that the opposite of a thing is part of it. Secondly, because the discussion is about the non-existence of absolute non-existence, and that it is a part of absolute non-existence and its opposite, so the statement that it is not a part of the absolutely non-existent has no bearing on what we are discussing. Thirdly, because the statement that non-existence exists in the mind has no meaning, because all non-existences are among the non-existent (ma'dūmāt), as the commentator (al-shāriḥ) stated in the discussion of the differentiation of non-existents; yes, after conception it exists in the mind, but the discussion here is about non-existence itself. Fourthly, because the statement that non-existence is not non-existent nor existent is only concerning absolute non-existence, and the discussion here is about the non-existence of absolute non-existence, which is a specific non-existence. His statement: (For the non-existence of absolute non-existence, etc.) means that this qualified (non-existence), inasmuch as it is non-existence qualified by a condition, without consideration of the specificity of the condition, is a type (naw') of it, and inasmuch as it is a raising of non-existence, it is opposite to it. So the perspective in the first consideration is that it is non-existence qualified by a condition, and in the second consideration it is the raising and negation of non-existence. So the position differs according to consideration. This is what some investigators (muḥaqqiqīn) have stated. His statement: (And union in identity) The author (al-muṣannif) said in the discussion of quiddity (al-māhiyya) and the meaning of predicating 'animal' of 'human': these two concepts, different in the mind, their external and estimative identity is one, so neither the unity of two nor the predication of a thing of itself follows. The commentator said: The aforementioned interpretation is not consistent in the case of 'human is blind,' for there is no identity for the concept of blind that is one with the identity of human, otherwise it would be externally existent. Therefore he diverted the text from its apparent meaning and interpreted it according to his preferred view, i.e., union in identity with respect to truth (ṣidq), not that the absolutely non-existent is meant as non-existent in mind and external, for non-existence exists in the mind, and because non-existence is not non-existent, otherwise the affirmation of a thing of itself would follow, just as it is also not existent. And the establishment of a middle term does not follow because non-existence does not admit this division, as was indicated in al-Tajrīd. 'Mu'ayyan' His statement: (Inasmuch as it is a raising of absolute non-existence) He meant by absolute non-existence the non-existence not attributed to anything, not non-existence in mind and external, i.e., the non-existence of mental and external existence. Just as by absolutely non-existent is meant the non-existent in both, otherwise it would not be correct that specific non-existence is a part of it, for it is not truly described as the non-existence of absolute existence, but rather it is the non-existence of non-existence. If you say: A part of a thing is an affirmation of it, not a raising, and also the raising of non-existence is existence, and it is not by self-evidence a part of non-existence. I say: Part-ness (qismiyya) is affirmation with respect to essence (dhāt), and opposite-ness (qasīmiyya) is raising with respect to concept (mafhūm). Then the raising of non-existence entails existence, not itself. And even if the commentator's words in the discussion of opposition and implication suggest that, it does not harm part-ness. The Fourth Observatory - On Proving Necessary Sciences 'Blackness is blackness' and not the judgment of the unity of the two. So this is a reply to the second proof in the first branch, which is the side of affirmation from the first disjunction of the third aspect, namely his statement: 'And also, it is a judgment of the unity of the two,' and he left the reply to the first proof in this branch, namely his statement: 'It is in itself non-existent,' etc., relying on what will come that quiddity in its essence is neither existent nor non-existent. Its identity is identical to its identity, but al-Muḥaqqiq al-Dawwānī, quoting from al-Shaykh, said: 'Non-existential things predicated of a thing are accidentally (bi-l-'araḍ) united with it because they are abstracted from it, even if they are not truly united with it. So the interpretation of predication as union in identity applies to essential attributes (dhātiyyāt), accidental attributes ('araḍiyyāt), and non-existential things (al-umūr al-'adamiyya).' I say: Perhaps this is what is meant by union in truth (al-ittiḥād fī al-ṣidq), so the two interpretations are one. His statement: i.e., essence as truth upon it. If you say: Truth linked by 'upon' means predication, so predication would be taken in its interpretation. I say: This is an explanation of the aspect of its correctness. As for its interpretation, it is the judgment of union between two things. And by this it became clear that his interpretation as conceptual distinction and union in truth, as the commentator chose in what follows, is not correct. His statement: (So this is a reply to the proof) He meant by the first branch that what is being considered is the affirmation and negation of the thing in itself. His statement: i.e., his statement: 'And also,' etc., is an explanation of the second proof. And you have known in what preceded that the aforementioned disjunction by his statement: 'Either it is identical or other' is with respect to essence and concept. So the sum of the reply is: We do not concede the necessity of the judgment of the unity of the two given difference, because the impossible is only the judgment of the unity of the two inasmuch as they are two, and here it is not so, because the difference is with respect to concept, and the union is with respect to identity. And by this it became clear that the reply is not complete without clarifying the two aspects of difference and union. His statement: (That quiddity in its essence, etc.) is based on the fact that neither of them is identical to it nor included in it, so both are negated from it in its rank, and the removal of the two opposites in a rank is permissible. And if it is not in itself non-existent, then from the subsistence of existence by it, the subsistence of the existent by the non-existent does not follow. His statement: (And not the judgment of the unity of the two) i.e., the corrupt union, which is the union of two things in essence. As for the union of two things which are the two different concepts with respect to essence, there is no harm in it. His statement: (So this is a reply to the second proof in the first branch) He meant by the first branch that what is intended in it is the affirmation of the thing and its negation in itself. Then that what was mentioned is a reply to what was mentioned only if the arguer by 'selfness' and 'otherness' means with respect to external reality. For if that were intended, then it would be a reply to the refutation of selfness, and the estimation would be: 'And predication only benefits conceptual difference.' But his statement: 'And union in identity' would then not be free from an aspect of redundancy, unless it is interpreted as removing a misconception. So the more apparent is that selfness is intended with respect to essence and concept, and by 'their otherness' is intended otherness with respect to their sum, not with respect to each of them. So reflect. His statement: (Based on what will come that quiddity in its essence is neither existent nor non-existent). It is said against it: The meaning of this statement is that neither of them is identical to it nor included in it, not that it is in itself separate from either of them.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from Sharh al-Mawaqif, likely on a previous text, discussing the nature of absolute non-existence and the predication of existence. The commentator begins by analyzing the concept of 'the non-existence of absolute non-existence' (عدـ العدم المطلق). He explains that this entity can be viewed in two ways: as a raising (رفع) of absolute non-existence, making it an opposite (قسيم) of absolute non-existence; and as a specific non-existence (عدم خاص) in itself, making it a part (قسم) of absolute non-existence. This dual aspect is crucial to understanding how it relates to absolute non-existence. Then he discusses predication (الحمل), specifically the predication of 'existent' upon 'blackness'. He states that such a predication is valid only because there is conceptual distinction (التغاير مفهوماً) between the two concepts, but they are united in identity (هوية) in the thing itself. This avoids the problem of lack of benefit (عدم الإفادة) that would occur if the concepts were identical. He then addresses objections: that non-existence does not admit division, that non-existence is not a thing, that non-existence exists in the mind, and that non-existence is neither existent nor non-existent. He refutes these by distinguishing between absolute non-existence and specific non-existence, and by clarifying that the discussion is about the latter. Next, he comments on the phrase 'إذ يكون عدم العدم المطلق' explaining that the qualified non-existence is a type of absolute non-existence when considered as a qualified concept, and an opposite when considered as a raising of non-existence. He then moves to a discussion of union in identity (الاتحاد هوية) in predication. The author (المصنف) gave the example of predication 'animal' of 'human', saying the two concepts are distinct but their identity in the external and estimative realms is one. The commentator (الشارح) objects that this interpretation fails for negative predications like 'human is blind' because 'blind' has no external identity; thus he revises the interpretation to mean union in truth (الصدق) rather than real identity. The commentator then engages with al-Muḥaqqiq al-Dawwānī's quotation from al-Shaykh that non-existential attributes are accidentally united with the subject. He suggests that both interpretations (union in identity and union in truth) converge. Finally, he responds to an argument about the judgment of the unity of two, explaining that the reply requires clarifying that difference is conceptual while union is in identity, and he discusses the nuances of the arguer's intentions regarding 'selfness' and 'otherness'. The passage concludes with a comment on quiddity (الماهية) being neither existent nor non-existent in itself, and a clarification that this does not mean quiddity is separate from both but that neither is identical to it or included in it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the non-existence of absolute non-existence is simply existence itself.
Correction: It is actually a specific non-existence that is both a part and an opposite of absolute non-existence; it entails existence but is not identical to it.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why is 'the non-existence of absolute non-existence' both a part (قسم) and an opposite (قسيم) of absolute non-existence?
A) Because it is identical to absolute non-existence in one respect and different in another.
B) Because it is considered absolutely whereas absolute non-existence is considered specifically.
C) Because in one respect it is a raising of absolute non-existence (making it opposite), and in another respect it is a specific non-existence (making it a part).
D) Because it is a mental concept while absolute non-existence is external.

Key Takeaway
The non-existence of absolute non-existence is both a part and an opposite of absolute non-existence, depending on whether it is considered as a specific non-existence or as a raising of absolute non-existence.

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
8 min
Predication and Union in Identity
Commentary on predication and the interpretation of union in identity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations of predication: as conceptual distinction with union in identity, and as union in truth (ṣidq), and why the latter is preferred for non-existential attributes.

Core Idea
Predication requires conceptual distinction but existential union in the subject.

This passage delves into the mechanism of predication, especially when existence is predicated of non-existential things like blackness. The commentator explains that such predication is valid because there is conceptual distinction (التغاير مفهوماً) between the two concepts even though they are united in identity (هوية) in reality. He then critiques this view and offers an alternative interpretation based on union in truth (الصدق), which better accounts for negative predicates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Focus on the two interpretations of 'union in identity'.
Watch for the terms 'التغاير مفهوماً' and 'الاتحاد هوية'.
2
Identify the problem with the first interpretation
The commentator gives an example: 'human is blind.' The concept 'blind' has no external identity to unite with 'human'. Understand why this breaks the first interpretation.
3
Learn the alternative interpretation
The commentator revises 'union in identity' to mean 'union in truth (الصدق)', i.e., the predicate is true of the subject. See how this solves the problem.
Note the reference to al-Dawwānī and al-Shaykh.
4
Compare the two interpretations
The passage concludes that both interpretations converge because union in truth is what is meant by union in identity. Ask yourself: why is this distinction important for kalam?
This connects to the broader debate on the nature of attributes.

الثاني في الشق الأول الذي هو طرف الثبوت من الترديد الأول من الوجه الثالث أعني قوله : وأيضاً فإنه حكم بوحدة الاثنين وترك جواب الدليل الأول في هذا الشق أعني قوله: فهو في نفسه معدوم إلخ اعتمادا على ما سيجيء من أن الماهية في حد ذاتها ليست موجودة ولا معدومة هويته عين هويته، لكن قال المحقق الدواني ناقلاً عن الشيخ : إن الأمور العدمية المحمولة على الشيء متحدة معه بالعرض لكونها منتزعة منه وإن لم تكن متحدة معه حقيقة فتفسير الحمل بالاتحاد بالهوية جار في الذاتيات والعرضيات والأمور العدمية أقول ولعل هذا هو المراد بالاتحاد في الصدق فمرجع التفسيرين واحد. قوله : أي ذاتاً صدقاً عليه فإن قلت الصدق الموصول بعلى معناه الحمل، فيلزم أخذ الحمل في تفسيره قلت : هذا بيان لوجه صحته، وأما تفسيره فهو الحكم بالاتحاد بين الشيئين، وبهذا ظهر أن تفسيره بالتغاير في المفهوم الاتحاد في الصدق كما اختار الشارح فيما سيأتي غير صحيح. قوله : (فهذا جواب عن الدليل أراد بالشق الأول أن يكون المتردد فيه ثبوت الشيء وعدمه في نفسه وقوله : أعني قوله: وأيضاً إلخ بيان للدليل الثاني، وقد عرفت فيما سبق أن الترديد المذكور بقوله : إما نفسه أو غيره بحسب الذات والمفهوم فحاصل الجواب : أن لا نسلم لزوم الحكم بوحدة الاثنين على تقدير المغايرة، لأن المحال إنما هو الحكم بوحدة الاثنين من حيث إنهما اثنان وهاهنا ليس كذلك لأن التغاير من حيث المفهوم، والاتحاد من حيث الهوية، وبهذا ظهر أنه لا يتم الجواب بدون بيان جهتي التغاير والاتحاد . قوله : ( من أن الماهية في حد ذاتها إلخ) بناء على أن شيئاً منهما ليس نفسها ولا داخلاً فيها، فهما مسلوبان عنها في مرتبتها وارتفاع النقيضين في المرتبة جائز، وإذا لم تكن في نفسها معدومة لم يلزم من قيام الوجود بها قيام الموجود بالمعدوم . قوله : ( ولا الحكم بوحدة الاثنين) أي الاتحاد الفاسد وهو اتحاد الاثنين ذاتاً، وأما اتحاد الاثنين اللذين هما المفهومان المتغايران بحسب الذات فلا محذور فيه . قوله : (فهذا جواب عن الدليل الثاني في الشق الأول أراد بالشق الأول أن يكون المراد فيه ثبوت الشيء وعدمه في نفسه، ثم أن كون ما ذكر جواباً عما ذكر، إنما هو إذا لم يكن مراد المستدل بالنفسية والغيرية هما بحسب الخارج، إذ لو أريد ذلك لكان جواباً عن إبطال النفسية، ويكون التقدير والحمل، إنما أفاد التغاير مفهوماً، لكن قوله : والاتحاد هوية لا يخلو عن شائبة اللغوية حينئذ إلا أن يحمل على دفع وهم، فالأظهر أن يراد النفسية بحسب الذات والمفهوم ویراد بغيريتهما الغيرية بحسب مجموعهما لا بحسب كل منهما فتدبر. قوله : ( على ما سيجيء من أن الماهية في حد ذاتها ليست موجودة ولا معدومة قيل : عليه معنى هذا الكلام أن أحدهما ليس عينها ولا داخلاً فيها، لا أنها في نفسها منفكة عن أحدهما،
The Fourth Observatory - On Proving Necessary Sciences. Concerning the raising of the reality of non-existence, and there is no impossibility in it either, for the non-existence of absolute non-existence, inasmuch as it is a raising of absolute non-existence, is its opposite (qasīm), and inasmuch as it is a specific non-existence, it is a part (qism) of it. (And predication) i.e., the predication of existent upon blackness is only correct (due to conceptual distinction) for the concept of blackness is different from the concept of existent, and the union (al-ittiḥād) is in identity (huwiyya) i.e., essence (dhāt) in truth (ṣidqan) upon it, so there is no lack of benefit here, as in our statement: 'It is not existent also,' and the establishment of a middle term does not follow because non-existence does not admit this division, and it is not a thing. Firstly, because the expression does not support it, for what is fitting then is not that the opposite of a thing is part of it. Secondly, because the discussion is about the non-existence of absolute non-existence, and that it is a part of absolute non-existence and its opposite, so the statement that it is not a part of the absolutely non-existent has no bearing on what we are discussing. Thirdly, because the statement that non-existence exists in the mind has no meaning, because all non-existences are among the non-existent (ma'dūmāt), as the commentator (al-shāriḥ) stated in the discussion of the differentiation of non-existents; yes, after conception it exists in the mind, but the discussion here is about non-existence itself. Fourthly, because the statement that non-existence is not non-existent nor existent is only concerning absolute non-existence, and the discussion here is about the non-existence of absolute non-existence, which is a specific non-existence. His statement: (For the non-existence of absolute non-existence, etc.) means that this qualified (non-existence), inasmuch as it is non-existence qualified by a condition, without consideration of the specificity of the condition, is a type (naw') of it, and inasmuch as it is a raising of non-existence, it is opposite to it. So the perspective in the first consideration is that it is non-existence qualified by a condition, and in the second consideration it is the raising and negation of non-existence. So the position differs according to consideration. This is what some investigators (muḥaqqiqīn) have stated. His statement: (And union in identity) The author (al-muṣannif) said in the discussion of quiddity (al-māhiyya) and the meaning of predicating 'animal' of 'human': these two concepts, different in the mind, their external and estimative identity is one, so neither the unity of two nor the predication of a thing of itself follows. The commentator said: The aforementioned interpretation is not consistent in the case of 'human is blind,' for there is no identity for the concept of blind that is one with the identity of human, otherwise it would be externally existent. Therefore he diverted the text from its apparent meaning and interpreted it according to his preferred view, i.e., union in identity with respect to truth (ṣidq), not that the absolutely non-existent is meant as non-existent in mind and external, for non-existence exists in the mind, and because non-existence is not non-existent, otherwise the affirmation of a thing of itself would follow, just as it is also not existent. And the establishment of a middle term does not follow because non-existence does not admit this division, as was indicated in al-Tajrīd. 'Mu'ayyan' His statement: (Inasmuch as it is a raising of absolute non-existence) He meant by absolute non-existence the non-existence not attributed to anything, not non-existence in mind and external, i.e., the non-existence of mental and external existence. Just as by absolutely non-existent is meant the non-existent in both, otherwise it would not be correct that specific non-existence is a part of it, for it is not truly described as the non-existence of absolute existence, but rather it is the non-existence of non-existence. If you say: A part of a thing is an affirmation of it, not a raising, and also the raising of non-existence is existence, and it is not by self-evidence a part of non-existence. I say: Part-ness (qismiyya) is affirmation with respect to essence (dhāt), and opposite-ness (qasīmiyya) is raising with respect to concept (mafhūm). Then the raising of non-existence entails existence, not itself. And even if the commentator's words in the discussion of opposition and implication suggest that, it does not harm part-ness. The Fourth Observatory - On Proving Necessary Sciences 'Blackness is blackness' and not the judgment of the unity of the two. So this is a reply to the second proof in the first branch, which is the side of affirmation from the first disjunction of the third aspect, namely his statement: 'And also, it is a judgment of the unity of the two,' and he left the reply to the first proof in this branch, namely his statement: 'It is in itself non-existent,' etc., relying on what will come that quiddity in its essence is neither existent nor non-existent. Its identity is identical to its identity, but al-Muḥaqqiq al-Dawwānī, quoting from al-Shaykh, said: 'Non-existential things predicated of a thing are accidentally (bi-l-'araḍ) united with it because they are abstracted from it, even if they are not truly united with it. So the interpretation of predication as union in identity applies to essential attributes (dhātiyyāt), accidental attributes ('araḍiyyāt), and non-existential things (al-umūr al-'adamiyya).' I say: Perhaps this is what is meant by union in truth (al-ittiḥād fī al-ṣidq), so the two interpretations are one. His statement: i.e., essence as truth upon it. If you say: Truth linked by 'upon' means predication, so predication would be taken in its interpretation. I say: This is an explanation of the aspect of its correctness. As for its interpretation, it is the judgment of union between two things. And by this it became clear that his interpretation as conceptual distinction and union in truth, as the commentator chose in what follows, is not correct. His statement: (So this is a reply to the proof) He meant by the first branch that what is being considered is the affirmation and negation of the thing in itself. His statement: i.e., his statement: 'And also,' etc., is an explanation of the second proof. And you have known in what preceded that the aforementioned disjunction by his statement: 'Either it is identical or other' is with respect to essence and concept. So the sum of the reply is: We do not concede the necessity of the judgment of the unity of the two given difference, because the impossible is only the judgment of the unity of the two inasmuch as they are two, and here it is not so, because the difference is with respect to concept, and the union is with respect to identity. And by this it became clear that the reply is not complete without clarifying the two aspects of difference and union. His statement: (That quiddity in its essence, etc.) is based on the fact that neither of them is identical to it nor included in it, so both are negated from it in its rank, and the removal of the two opposites in a rank is permissible. And if it is not in itself non-existent, then from the subsistence of existence by it, the subsistence of the existent by the non-existent does not follow. His statement: (And not the judgment of the unity of the two) i.e., the corrupt union, which is the union of two things in essence. As for the union of two things which are the two different concepts with respect to essence, there is no harm in it. His statement: (So this is a reply to the second proof in the first branch) He meant by the first branch that what is intended in it is the affirmation of the thing and its negation in itself. Then that what was mentioned is a reply to what was mentioned only if the arguer by 'selfness' and 'otherness' means with respect to external reality. For if that were intended, then it would be a reply to the refutation of selfness, and the estimation would be: 'And predication only benefits conceptual difference.' But his statement: 'And union in identity' would then not be free from an aspect of redundancy, unless it is interpreted as removing a misconception. So the more apparent is that selfness is intended with respect to essence and concept, and by 'their otherness' is intended otherness with respect to their sum, not with respect to each of them. So reflect. His statement: (Based on what will come that quiddity in its essence is neither existent nor non-existent). It is said against it: The meaning of this statement is that neither of them is identical to it nor included in it, not that it is in itself separate from either of them.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from Sharh al-Mawaqif, likely on a previous text, discussing the nature of absolute non-existence and the predication of existence. The commentator begins by analyzing the concept of 'the non-existence of absolute non-existence' (عدـ العدم المطلق). He explains that this entity can be viewed in two ways: as a raising (رفع) of absolute non-existence, making it an opposite (قسيم) of absolute non-existence; and as a specific non-existence (عدم خاص) in itself, making it a part (قسم) of absolute non-existence. This dual aspect is crucial to understanding how it relates to absolute non-existence. Then he discusses predication (الحمل), specifically the predication of 'existent' upon 'blackness'. He states that such a predication is valid only because there is conceptual distinction (التغاير مفهوماً) between the two concepts, but they are united in identity (هوية) in the thing itself. This avoids the problem of lack of benefit (عدم الإفادة) that would occur if the concepts were identical. He then addresses objections: that non-existence does not admit division, that non-existence is not a thing, that non-existence exists in the mind, and that non-existence is neither existent nor non-existent. He refutes these by distinguishing between absolute non-existence and specific non-existence, and by clarifying that the discussion is about the latter. Next, he comments on the phrase 'إذ يكون عدم العدم المطلق' explaining that the qualified non-existence is a type of absolute non-existence when considered as a qualified concept, and an opposite when considered as a raising of non-existence. He then moves to a discussion of union in identity (الاتحاد هوية) in predication. The author (المصنف) gave the example of predication 'animal' of 'human', saying the two concepts are distinct but their identity in the external and estimative realms is one. The commentator (الشارح) objects that this interpretation fails for negative predications like 'human is blind' because 'blind' has no external identity; thus he revises the interpretation to mean union in truth (الصدق) rather than real identity. The commentator then engages with al-Muḥaqqiq al-Dawwānī's quotation from al-Shaykh that non-existential attributes are accidentally united with the subject. He suggests that both interpretations (union in identity and union in truth) converge. Finally, he responds to an argument about the judgment of the unity of two, explaining that the reply requires clarifying that difference is conceptual while union is in identity, and he discusses the nuances of the arguer's intentions regarding 'selfness' and 'otherness'. The passage concludes with a comment on quiddity (الماهية) being neither existent nor non-existent in itself, and a clarification that this does not mean quiddity is separate from both but that neither is identical to it or included in it.

التغاير مفهوماً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator reject the original interpretation of 'union in identity' for the predicate 'blind' in 'human is blind'?
A) Because 'blind' and 'human' are not conceptually distinct.
B) Because 'blind' has no external identity to share with 'human'.
C) Because 'blind' is a non-existential attribute and cannot be predicated.
D) Because the original interpretation leads to the unity of two.

Key Takeaway
Predication of non-existential attributes requires union in truth (الصدق) rather than union in identity (هوية), because the predicate may lack external existence.

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
8 min
Reply to the Unity-of-Two Argument and Quiddity
Commentary on replying to the unity-of-two argument and the nature of quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reply to the argument that predication implies the unity of two, and the role of quiddity being neither existent nor non-existent in itself.

Core Idea
Predication does not imply corrupt unity because difference and union are in different respects.

This passage continues the discussion of predication by addressing a specific argument: if subject and predicate are distinct, then predication would assert the unity of two, which is impossible. The commentator explains that the difference is conceptual while the union is in identity, so no corrupt unity follows. He also clarifies that quiddity in itself is neither existent nor non-existent, which avoids the problem of predicating existence of something non-existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the terms 'نفسية' and 'غيرية', and the discussion of quiddity.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the argument being refuted
The argument claims that predication of existence on a thing forces the unity of two distinct concepts. Find where this is stated.
3
Understand the reply
The reply: difference is in concept (مفهوماً), union is in identity (هوية). So the two are not two in every respect, avoiding corrupt unity.
This is a standard kalam move.
4
Learn the role of quiddity
The commentator also relies on the idea that quiddity in itself is neither existent nor non-existent. This prevents the need to predicate existence of a non-existent thing.
This will be expanded in later lessons.
5
Reflect on the bigger picture
Ask yourself: how does this reply protect the validity of predication in theological discourse?
Connects to proving necessary sciences.

المرصد الرابع - في إثبات العلوم الضرورية في رفع حقيقة العدم، ولا استحالة فيه أيضاً، إذ يكون عدم العدم المطلق من حيث أنه رفع للعدم المطلق قسيماً له، ومن حيث أنه عدم خاص قسماً منه، ( والحمل) أي حمل الموجود على السواد إنما صح ( للتغاير مفهوماً فإن مفهوم السواد مغاير لمفهوم الموجود والاتحاد (هوية) أي ذاتاً صدقاً عليه فلا يلزم هاهنا عدم الإفادة، كما في قولنا : أنه ليس بموجود أيضاً، ولا يلزم ثبوت الواسطة لأن العدم لا يقبل هذه القسمة، وليس بشيء أما أولاً فلان العبارة لا تساعده، إذ اللائق حينئذ وليس في ذلك كون قسيم الشيء قسماً منه، وأما ثانياً فلان الكلام في عدم العدم المطلق، وأنه قسم من العدم المطلق، وقسيم له فالقول : بأنه ليس قسماً من المعدوم المطلق لا دخل له فيما نحن فيه، وأما ثالثاً فلان القول : بأن العدم موجود في الذهن مما لا معنی له، لأن الأعدام كلها من جملة المعدومات، كما صرح به الشارح في بحث تمايز المعدومات نعم إنه بعد التصور موجود في الذهن والكلام هاهنا في نفس العدم، وأما رابعاً فلان القول : بأن العدم ليس بمعدوم ولا موجود إنما هو في العدم المطلق، والكلام هاهنا في عدم العدم المطلق وهو عدم خاص. قوله : (إذ يكون عدم العدم المطلق إلخ) يعني أن هذا المقيد من حيث إنه عدم مقيد مع قطع النظر عن خصوصية القيد نوع منه ومن حيث إنه رفع للعدم مقابل له، فالمنظور في الاعتبار الأول كونه عدماً مقيداً بقيد وحينئذ الاعتبار الثاني هو كونه رفع العدم وسلبه، فالموضع مختلف باعتبار كذا أفاده بعض المحققين. قوله ( والاتحاد هوبة) قال المصنف في بحث الماهية ومعنى حمل الحيوان على الإنسان هذين المفهومين المتغايرين في العقل هويتهما الخارجية والوهمية واحدة، فلا يلزم وحدة الاثنين ولا حمل الشيء على نفسه، وقال الشارح: إن التفسير المذكور لا يطرد في نحو الإنسان أعمى، إذ لا هوية لمفهوم الأعمى متحدة مع هوية الإنسان وإلا لكان موجوداً خارجياً، فلهذا صرف المتن عن ظاهره وفسره بما هو المختار عنده أي الاتحاد هوية باعتبار الصدق لا أن المعدوم المطلق المراد به المعدوم في الذهن والخارج، إذ العدم موجود في الذهن ولأن العدم ليس بمعدوم، وإلا لزم ثبوت الشيء لنفسه كما أنه ليس بموجود أيضاً، ولا يلزم ثبوت الواسطة لأن العدم لا يقبل هذه القسمة كما أشير إليه في التجريد معين قوله : ( من حيث إنه رفع للعدم المطلق أراد بالعدم المطلق العدم الغير المضاف إلى شيء لا العدم في الذهن والخارج أي عدم الوجود الذهني والخارجي، كما أن المراد بالعدم المطلق المعدوم فيهما وإلا لم يصح كون العدم الخاص قسما منه، إذ لا يصدق عليه أنه عدم الوجود المطلق، بل هو عدم العدم فإن قلت: قسم الشيء مثبت له لا رافع، وأيضاً رفع العدم وجود وهو لا يكون قسماً من العدم بالبداهة قلت القسمية الإثبات بحسب الذات، والقسيمية الرفع بحسب المفهوم، ثم سيجيء من أن الماهية في حد ذاتها ليست موجودة ولا معدومة هويته عين هويته، لكن قال المحقق الدواني ناقلاً عن الشيخ : إن الأمور العدمية المحمولة على الشيء متحدة معه بالعرض لكونها منتزعة منه وإن لم تكن متحدة معه حقيقة فتفسير الحمل بالاتحاد بالهوية جار في الذاتيات والعرضيات والأمور العدمية أقول ولعل هذا هو المراد بالاتحاد في الصدق فمرجع التفسيرين واحد. قوله : أي ذاتاً صدقاً عليه فإن قلت الصدق الموصول بعلى معناه الحمل، فيلزم أخذ الحمل في تفسيره قلت : هذا بيان لوجه صحته، وأما تفسيره فهو الحكم بالاتحاد بين الشيئين، وبهذا ظهر أن تفسيره بالتغاير في المفهوم الاتحاد في الصدق كما اختار الشارح فيما سيأتي غير صحيح. قوله : (فهذا جواب عن الدليل أراد بالشق الأول أن يكون المتردد فيه ثبوت الشيء وعدمه في نفسه وقوله : أعني قوله: وأيضاً إلخ بيان للدليل الثاني، وقد عرفت فيما سبق أن الترديد المذكور بقوله : إما المفهوم، والاتحاد من حيث الهوية، وبهذا ظهر أنه لا يتم الجواب بدون بيان جهتي التغاير والاتحاد . قوله : ( من أن الماهية في حد ذاتها إلخ) بناء على أن شيئاً منهما ليس نفسها ولا داخلاً فيها، فهما مسلوبان ذاتاً، وأما اتحاد الاثنين اللذين هما المفهومان المتغايران بحسب الذات فلا محذور فيه . قوله : (فهذا جواب عن الدليل الثاني في الشق الأول أراد بالشق الأول أن يكون المراد فيه ثبوت الشيء وعدمه في نفسه، ثم أن كون ما ذكر جواباً عما ذكر، إنما هو إذا لم يكن مراد المستدل بالنفسية والغيرية هما بحسب الخارج، إذ لو أريد ذلك لكان جواباً عن إبطال النفسية، ويكون التقدير والحمل، إنما أفاد التغاير مفهوماً، لكن قوله : والاتحاد هوية لا يخلو عن شائبة اللغوية حينئذ إلا أن يحمل على دفع وهم، فالأظهر أن يراد النفسية بحسب الذات والمفهوم ویراد بغيريتهما الغيرية بحسب مجموعهما لا بحسب كل منهما فتدبر. قوله : ( على ما سيجيء The Fourth Observatory - On Proving Necessary Sciences. Concerning the raising of the reality of non-existence, and there is no impossibility in it either, for the non-existence of absolute non-existence, inasmuch as it is a raising of absolute non-existence, is its opposite (qasīm), and inasmuch as it is a specific non-existence, it is a part (qism) of it. (And predication) i.e., the predication of existent upon blackness is only correct (due to conceptual distinction) for the concept of blackness is different from the concept of existent, and the union (al-ittiḥād) is in identity (huwiyya) i.e., essence (dhāt) in truth (ṣidqan) upon it, so there is no lack of benefit here, as in our statement: 'It is not existent also,' and the establishment of a middle term does not follow because non-existence does not admit this division, and it is not a thing. Firstly, because the expression does not support it, for what is fitting then is not that the opposite of a thing is part of it. Secondly, because the discussion is about the non-existence of absolute non-existence, and that it is a part of absolute non-existence and its opposite, so the statement that it is not a part of the absolutely non-existent has no bearing on what we are discussing. Thirdly, because the statement that non-existence exists in the mind has no meaning, because all non-existences are among the non-existent (ma'dūmāt), as the commentator (al-shāriḥ) stated in the discussion of the differentiation of non-existents; yes, after conception it exists in the mind, but the discussion here is about non-existence itself. Fourthly, because the statement that non-existence is not non-existent nor existent is only concerning absolute non-existence, and the discussion here is about the non-existence of absolute non-existence, which is a specific non-existence. His statement: (For the non-existence of absolute non-existence, etc.) means that this qualified (non-existence), inasmuch as it is non-existence qualified by a condition, without consideration of the specificity of the condition, is a type (naw') of it, and inasmuch as it is a raising of non-existence, it is opposite to it. So the perspective in the first consideration is that it is non-existence qualified by a condition, and in the second consideration it is the raising and negation of non-existence. So the position differs according to consideration. This is what some investigators (muḥaqqiqīn) have stated. His statement: (And union in identity) The author (al-muṣannif) said in the discussion of quiddity (al-māhiyya) and the meaning of predicating 'animal' of 'human': these two concepts, different in the mind, their external and estimative identity is one, so neither the unity of two nor the predication of a thing of itself follows. The commentator said: The aforementioned interpretation is not consistent in the case of 'human is blind,' for there is no identity for the concept of blind that is one with the identity of human, otherwise it would be externally existent. Therefore he diverted the text from its apparent meaning and interpreted it according to his preferred view, i.e., union in identity with respect to truth (ṣidq), not that the absolutely non-existent is meant as non-existent in mind and external, for non-existence exists in the mind, and because non-existence is not non-existent, otherwise the affirmation of a thing of itself would follow, just as it is also not existent. And the establishment of a middle term does not follow because non-existence does not admit this division, as was indicated in al-Tajrīd. 'Mu'ayyan' His statement: (Inasmuch as it is a raising of absolute non-existence) He meant by absolute non-existence the non-existence not attributed to anything, not non-existence in mind and external, i.e., the non-existence of mental and external existence. Just as by absolutely non-existent is meant the non-existent in both, otherwise it would not be correct that specific non-existence is a part of it, for it is not truly described as the non-existence of absolute existence, but rather it is the non-existence of non-existence. If you say: A part of a thing is an affirmation of it, not a raising, and also the raising of non-existence is existence, and it is not by self-evidence a part of non-existence. I say: Part-ness (qismiyya) is affirmation with respect to essence (dhāt), and opposite-ness (qasīmiyya) is raising with respect to concept (mafhūm). Then the raising of non-existence entails existence, not itself. And even if the commentator's words in the discussion of opposition and implication suggest that, it does not harm part-ness. The Fourth Observatory - On Proving Necessary Sciences 'Blackness is blackness' and not the judgment of the unity of the two. So this is a reply to the second proof in the first branch, which is the side of affirmation from the first disjunction of the third aspect, namely his statement: 'And also, it is a judgment of the unity of the two,' and he left the reply to the first proof in this branch, namely his statement: 'It is in itself non-existent,' etc., relying on what will come that quiddity in its essence is neither existent nor non-existent. Its identity is identical to its identity, but al-Muḥaqqiq al-Dawwānī, quoting from al-Shaykh, said: 'Non-existential things predicated of a thing are accidentally (bi-l-'araḍ) united with it because they are abstracted from it, even if they are not truly united with it. So the interpretation of predication as union in identity applies to essential attributes (dhātiyyāt), accidental attributes ('araḍiyyāt), and non-existential things (al-umūr al-'adamiyya).' I say: Perhaps this is what is meant by union in truth (al-ittiḥād fī al-ṣidq), so the two interpretations are one. His statement: i.e., essence as truth upon it. If you say: Truth linked by 'upon' means predication, so predication would be taken in its interpretation. I say: This is an explanation of the aspect of its correctness. As for its interpretation, it is the judgment of union between two things. And by this it became clear that his interpretation as conceptual distinction and union in truth, as the commentator chose in what follows, is not correct. His statement: (So this is a reply to the proof) He meant by the first branch that what is being considered is the affirmation and negation of the thing in itself. His statement: i.e., his statement: 'And also,' etc., is an explanation of the second proof. And you have known in what preceded that the aforementioned disjunction by his statement: 'Either it is identical or other' is with respect to essence and concept. So the sum of the reply is: We do not concede the necessity of the judgment of the unity of the two given difference, because the impossible is only the judgment of the unity of the two inasmuch as they are two, and here it is not so, because the difference is with respect to concept, and the union is with respect to identity. And by this it became clear that the reply is not complete without clarifying the two aspects of difference and union. His statement: (That quiddity in its essence, etc.) is based on the fact that neither of them is identical to it nor included in it, so both are negated from it in its rank, and the removal of the two opposites in a rank is permissible. And if it is not in itself non-existent, then from the subsistence of existence by it, the subsistence of the existent by the non-existent does not follow. His statement: (And not the judgment of the unity of the two) i.e., the corrupt union, which is the union of two things in essence. As for the union of two things which are the two different concepts with respect to essence, there is no harm in it. His statement: (So this is a reply to the second proof in the first branch) He meant by the first branch that what is intended in it is the affirmation of the thing and its negation in itself. Then that what was mentioned is a reply to what was mentioned only if the arguer by 'selfness' and 'otherness' means with respect to external reality. For if that were intended, then it would be a reply to the refutation of selfness, and the estimation would be: 'And predication only benefits conceptual difference.' But his statement: 'And union in identity' would then not be free from an aspect of redundancy, unless it is interpreted as removing a misconception. So the more apparent is that selfness is intended with respect to essence and concept, and by 'their otherness' is intended otherness with respect to their sum, not with respect to each of them. So reflect. His statement: (Based on what will come that quiddity in its essence is neither existent nor non-existent). It is said against it: The meaning of this statement is that neither of them is identical to it nor included in it, not that it is in itself separate from either of them.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from Sharh al-Mawaqif, likely on a previous text, discussing the nature of absolute non-existence and the predication of existence. The commentator begins by analyzing the concept of 'the non-existence of absolute non-existence' (عدـ العدم المطلق). He explains that this entity can be viewed in two ways: as a raising (رفع) of absolute non-existence, making it an opposite (قسيم) of absolute non-existence; and as a specific non-existence (عدم خاص) in itself, making it a part (قسم) of absolute non-existence. This dual aspect is crucial to understanding how it relates to absolute non-existence. Then he discusses predication (الحمل), specifically the predication of 'existent' upon 'blackness'. He states that such a predication is valid only because there is conceptual distinction (التغاير مفهوماً) between the two concepts, but they are united in identity (هوية) in the thing itself. This avoids the problem of lack of benefit (عدم الإفادة) that would occur if the concepts were identical. He then addresses objections: that non-existence does not admit division, that non-existence is not a thing, that non-existence exists in the mind, and that non-existence is neither existent nor non-existent. He refutes these by distinguishing between absolute non-existence and specific non-existence, and by clarifying that the discussion is about the latter. Next, he comments on the phrase 'إذ يكون عدم العدم المطلق' explaining that the qualified non-existence is a type of absolute non-existence when considered as a qualified concept, and an opposite when considered as a raising of non-existence. He then moves to a discussion of union in identity (الاتحاد هوية) in predication. The author (المصنف) gave the example of predication 'animal' of 'human', saying the two concepts are distinct but their identity in the external and estimative realms is one. The commentator (الشارح) objects that this interpretation fails for negative predications like 'human is blind' because 'blind' has no external identity; thus he revises the interpretation to mean union in truth (الصدق) rather than real identity. The commentator then engages with al-Muḥaqqiq al-Dawwānī's quotation from al-Shaykh that non-existential attributes are accidentally united with the subject. He suggests that both interpretations (union in identity and union in truth) converge. Finally, he responds to an argument about the judgment of the unity of two, explaining that the reply requires clarifying that difference is conceptual while union is in identity, and he discusses the nuances of the arguer's intentions regarding 'selfness' and 'otherness'. The passage concludes with a comment on quiddity (الماهية) being neither existent nor non-existent in itself, and a clarification that this does not mean quiddity is separate from both but that neither is identical to it or included in it.

Checkpoint
What is the key reply to the argument that predication implies the unity of two distinct things?
A) The subject and predicate are actually the same thing.
B) The difference is conceptual while the union is in identity, so no corrupt unity follows.
C) Predication is not a real relation but a mental one.
D) The argument applies only to negative predicates.

Key Takeaway
Predication of existence on a quiddity does not imply the unity of two distinct entities because the difference is conceptual and the union is in identity; moreover, quiddity in itself is neutral to existence and non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is Knowing', do you truly believe that His Knowledge is not other than Him? Have you ever caught yourself thinking of His attributes as separate additions? Reflect on how this logical distinction challenges your mental imagery of divine unity. What step will you take today to correct any hidden assumption that separates Allah's attributes from His essence?
?
The argument says that difference is conceptual while union is in identity. In your own faith, where might you be making a conceptual distinction into a real separation? For instance, when you think of your own existence and your essence, do you sometimes forget that your existence is a gift from Allah, not part of your own self? How can you practice being more aware of Allah's oneness in your daily speech?
?
Consider the neutrality of quiddity: in itself, it is neither existent nor non-existent. Your own soul, before Allah created it, was nothing. Does that realization humble you? How does knowing that your existence is not your own change the way you live today?

The Fifth Doubt: Changing Certainty and Its Refutation
Al-Jurjani presents the fifth doubt and refutes it by distinguishing proper abstraction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why changing one's mind about a proof does not undermine the certainty of necessary sciences, and how proper abstraction preserves certainty.

Core Idea
Certainty is lost only when abstraction is flawed, not when knowledge itself is flawed.

This lesson tackles a common skeptical doubt: if we sometimes realize our earlier certainty was mistaken, how can we trust any necessary knowledge? Al-Jurjani presents the doubt, then refutes it by distinguishing between proper and improper abstraction. You will see how the response saves the foundations of necessary science without denying human fallibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt and its refutation
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the doubt and the response as a whole.
Notice the structure: first the doubt, then the response with explanatory notes.
2
Identify the doubt's core claim
The doubt claims that since we sometimes change our minds about proofs we were certain of, all necessary propositions could be uncertain. Pinpoint this reasoning in the text.
The doubt uses the example of changing between conflicting doctrines.
3
Understand the refutation's key distinction
The refutation states that the error lies in incomplete abstraction (tajrid) of the two extremes, not in the necessary knowledge itself. This distinction is crucial.
The respondent (manī') blocks the opponent's move unless the opponent proves the possibility of improper abstraction.
4
See why this preserves necessary knowledge
If abstraction is proper, certainty is secure. The doubt only highlights cases of flawed abstraction, not a general uncertainty. Reflect on this conclusion.
This aligns with the lesson's learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة ( الخامسة ) لهم ( أنا نجزم بصحة دليل آونة ) أي أزمنة متطاولة ( و ) نجزم لأجله ( بما يلزمه من النتيجة ثم يظهر لنا ( خطوه ) ظهوراً لا تبقى معه فيه شبهة ولذلك ننقل المذاهب) المتنافية وأدلتها المتخالفة، إذ ربما لاح حقية ما حكم فيها ببطلانه وبالعكس ( فجاز مثله في الكل) أي كل ما يجزم به من البديهيات فيرتفع الأمان عنها
قوله : ( فلا يلزم إلخ) وما قيل : احتمال عدم تجريد الطرفين كما هو حقه قائم في كل بديهي، إذ لا عبر بالجزم الحاصل فلا وثوق بشيء منها فخارج عن قانون المناظرة، لأن المجيب مانع فلا بد للخصم من إثبات الاحتمال المذكور.
قوله : (آونة) بالمد جمع أوان بمعنى الحين والحين الدهر والدهر الزمان الطويل كل ذلك في القاموس، فقيد التطاول مستفاد من لفظ ،آونة، وإنما لم يجعله بمعنى مطلق الوقت ترويجاً للشبهة.
قوله : ( ننقل المذاهب المتنافية أي من شخص واحد .
The fifth doubt they raise: 'We are certain of the correctness of a proof at times (i.e., long periods), and we are certain, because of it, of its necessary consequence; then its error appears to us in a way that leaves no doubt, and therefore we change from conflicting doctrines and their contradictory proofs, for perhaps the truth of what we had judged false becomes apparent, and vice versa.' So the like is possible in all that we are certain of from necessary propositions, and thus confidence in them is removed. His statement: 'So it does not necessarily follow, etc.' And it was said: the possibility that the two extremes were not abstracted as they should be exists in every necessary proposition, for no consideration is given to the actual certainty, so there is no trust in any of them, and this is outside the rules of debate, because the respondent is blocking, so the opponent must prove the mentioned possibility. His statement: 'Awinah' (plural of Awan, meaning time, long period) is from the lexicon: Awan means long time, so the restriction of length is derived from the word Awinah, and he did not make it mean absolute time in order to strengthen the doubt. His statement: 'We change from conflicting doctrines' i.e., by one person.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the fifth doubt against the certainty of necessary sciences. The doubt argues that since we sometimes change our minds about proofs after being certain (e.g., we switch between conflicting doctrines), the same uncertainty could apply to all necessary propositions. The response explains that when we change our minds, it is because the abstraction (tajrid) of the two extremes was not proper; the error lies in incomplete abstraction, not in the necessary knowledge itself. The commentator notes that this possibility of improper abstraction exists in every necessary proposition, but if the abstraction is correct, the certainty holds. Hence, the doubt does not undermine necessary knowledge itself.

Key Terms
شبهة خامسة
بديهيات
تجريد
أوان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Changing one's mind about a proof means the original certainty was false, so necessary sciences are unreliable.
Correction: The refutation shows that the change is due to incomplete abstraction, not a flaw in necessary knowledge itself. When abstraction is proper, certainty holds.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation of the fifth doubt, what is the reason we sometimes change our minds about a proof we were certain of?
A) The necessary proposition itself was uncertain.
B) The abstraction of the two extremes was not properly done.
C) Time erodes the certainty of all knowledge.
D) The proof was not from the necessary sciences.

Key Takeaway
The fifth doubt is refuted by showing that loss of certainty results from incomplete abstraction, not from the inherent unreliability of necessary knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you were absolutely certain about a belief, but later realized you were wrong. Did the error lie in the knowledge itself, or in how you abstracted it? What does this teach you about the foundations of your current certainties?
?
You claim to be certain about the truth of Islam. Is that certainty based on proper abstraction of evidence, or could there be unnoticed flaws? How can you ensure your abstraction is complete?
?
The passage argues that incomplete abstraction leads to changing certainty. How many of your religious convictions are the result of proper abstraction, and how many are inherited without inspection? What will you do today to test the abstraction of a key belief?

The Sixth Doubt and the First Example: Ethical Judgments
Al-Jurjani presents the sixth doubt and the first example from the Mu'tazila on ethical judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth doubt against necessary sciences and why the Mu'tazilite claim about good and bad being self-evident is actually a well-known (mashhurat) proposition, not a primary self-evident one.

Core Idea
Disagreement about self-evidence does not destroy certainty; the disputed examples are not genuinely self-evident.

Al-Jurjani presents the sixth doubt: if different schools each claim self-evidence for conflicting propositions, then all necessary knowledge would be suspect. To illustrate, he gives two examples from Mu'tazila-Ash'ari debates. In this lesson, we examine the first example: the judgment that truthful beneficial speech is good and harmful lying is bad. The Mu'tazila claim this is self-evident, but the Ash'aris and philosophers classify it as a well-known (mashhurat) proposition, which may be false. This shows that the doubt does not apply to genuinely self-evident propositions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how the doubt is introduced and the first example is presented.
Pay attention to the structure: doubt statement, then example, then response.
2
Identify the three positions
The Mu'tazila claim self-evidence; the Ash'aris and philosophers deny it and classify it as mashhurat.
Understand why classification matters.
3
Understand the implication
Why does this not undermine genuine necessary sciences? Because the disputed propositions are not truly self-evident.
This is the key to refuting the doubt.
4
Connect to the broader argument
This example illustrates that alleged self-evident propositions can actually be conventional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة (السادسة) لهم أن في كل مذهب) من المذاهب المشهورة (قضايا يدعي صاحبه فيها البداهة ومخالفوه ينكرونها ) أي البداهة في تلك القضايا (وهو) أي ما ذكر من ادعاء البداهة فيها وإنكارها ( يوجب الاشتباه في البديهيات بأسرها ( ورفع الأمان ) عنها ( فلنعد عدة منها ) أي من القضايا التي وقع النزاع في بداهتها ( الأولى للمعتزلة الصدق النافع حسن، والكذب الضار قبيح قالوا : يحكم بذلك بديهة العقل ) وأنكره الأشاعرة والحكماء ) واتفقوا على أنها ليست من القضايا الأولية بل من المشهورات التي قد تكون كاذبة وقد تكون صادقة ( الثانية لهم) أيضاً فإنهم قالوا: (العبد) (موجد) بالاستقلال ( لأفعاله) الاختيارية متمكن من فعلها وتركها بيده زمام الاختيار فيها وادعى بعضهم أن هذا الحكم بديهي (وهما ) أي الأشاعرة والحكماء (منعاه ) أي كذبا هذا الحكم ) وعارضاه ) أي قابلا ادعاء الضرورة فيه ) بضرورة أخرى في أنه لا بد له أي للفعل الصادر عن العبد من مرجح) يرجح أحد طرفيه الجائزين على الآخر، فإن حركته يمنة ويسرة إذا كانتا جائزتين منه على سواء، فلا بد بالضرورة في صدور إحداهما عنه من مرجح يرجحها على الأخرى ( فهو ) أي فذلك المرجح ( من خارج ) أي لا يكون صادرا عن العبد ) وإلا تسلسل ما صدر صدر عنه من أفعاله إلى ما لا يتناهى، بل ذلك
والذم فإنهما بهذا المعنى هو المتنازع فيه بين الفريقين لا بمعنى كونهما صفة كمال أو صفة نقصان، أو كونهما ملائما للغرض وغير ملائم له الحاكم بهما العقل إما بديهة أو نظراً . قوله : ( وادعى بعضهم إلخ) وهو أبو الحسين البصري سواء كان ذلك مذهبه في الواقع أو قال به تلبيساً على أصحابه وتفصيله في الموقف الخامس.
قوله : (أي كذباً) أي المنع وكذا المعارضة هاهنا بالمعنى اللغوي لا الاصطلاحي، إذ لا دليل هاهنا .
قوله : (بل من المشهورات التي قد تكون كاذبة لأن المراد بالحسن في محل النزاع كونه مناطاً للثواب، وبالقبح كونه مناطاً للعقاب لا معنى الملاءمة والمنافرة، والعقل لا مدخل له في الثواب والعقاب وسيجيء التفصيل في الإلهيات .
قوله : ( وادعى بعضهم أن هذا الحكم بديهي فيه بحث أما أولاً فلان مدعي البديهة هو أبو الحسين البصري وهو لا يقول : بكون العبد موجداً لأفعاله على سبيل الاستقلال فضلاً عن البديهة في ذلك، بل القائل بذلك جمهور المعتزلة وهم لا يدعون البديهة فيه كل ذلك مذكور في الموقف الخامس، وأما ثانياً فلان الفلاسفة يوافقون أبا الحسين في مذهبه كما صرح به في الإلهيات فكيف عدوا هاهنا مخالفين له؟ والحق أن ما ذكره هاهنا مبني على ظاهر ما نقل عن أبي الحسين من ادعاء الضرورة في استقلال العبد تلبيساً للأمر على سائر المعتزلة، كيلا يظنوا رجوعه عن مذهبهم كما أشار إليه في الإلهيات أو أن مدعى البداهة غيره وإن لم يذكر في هذا الكتاب والله أعلم .
قوله : (أي) (قابلاً) إشارة إلى أن المعارضة ليست على ظاهرها لأنها إقامة الدليل على خلاف ما أقام عليه الخصم ومدعي الخصم أن الحكم ،ضروري وبهذا المعنى قوله: فيما بعد ويعارضونهم فلا تغفل .
The sixth doubt they raise: that in every well-known school there are propositions whose adherent claims self-evidence for them while his opponents deny it—i.e., deny the self-evidence of those propositions. This (i.e., the claim of self-evidence and its denial) causes confusion about all self-evident propositions and removes confidence in them. So let us count a number of them (i.e., propositions about which there is disagreement regarding their self-evidence). The first: for the Mu'tazila, "truthful beneficial speech is good, and harmful lying is bad." They said: reason judges this self-evidently. But the Ash'aris and the philosophers denied it and agreed that these are not primary propositions but rather well-known (mashhurat) propositions which may be false or true. The second: also for them (the Mu'tazila), they said: "The servant is the independent creator of his voluntary acts, capable of doing and leaving them, the reins of choice in his hand," and some claimed that this judgment is self-evident. But the two groups (the Ash'aris and the philosophers) denied it—i.e., falsified this judgment—and countered it (i.e., accepted the claim of necessity in it) with another necessity: that the act that proceeds from the servant must have a preponderator that gives preponderance to one of its two possible aspects over the other. For if his movement to the right and left, when both are equally possible from him, then necessarily for the occurrence of one of them from him there must be a preponderator to preponderate it over the other. So that preponderator is from outside—i.e., not proceeding from the servant—otherwise there would be an infinite regress of acts proceeding from him. But that (the praise and blame) in this sense is what is disputed between the two groups, not in the sense of being attributes of perfection or deficiency, or being in harmony or discord with the purpose. The intellect judges them either self-evidently or speculatively. His statement: 'And some claimed, etc.'—He is Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, whether that was his actual doctrine or he said it to deceive his companions, and its detail is in the Fifth Observation. His statement: 'i.e., false'—meaning the denial and likewise the counter here are in the linguistic sense, not the technical, for there is no proof here. His statement: 'Rather they are from the well-known propositions that may be false because the intended meaning of 'good' in the disputed context is that it is the basis for reward, and 'bad' that it is the basis for punishment, not the meaning of harmony and discord. Intellect has no role in reward and punishment, and the detail will come in the Divine Things. His statement: 'And some claimed that this judgment is self-evident' is debatable. First, because the claimant of self-evidence is Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, and he does not say that the servant is the independent creator of his acts, let alone that this is self-evident; rather, the proponents of that are the majority of the Mu'tazila, and they do not claim self-evidence in it—all of that is mentioned in the Fifth Observation. Second, because the philosophers agree with Abu al-Husayn in his doctrine, as stated in the Divine Things, so how are they counted here as opposed to him? The truth is that what he mentioned here is based on the apparent meaning of what was transmitted from Abu al-Husayn of claiming necessity in the servant's independence in order to deceive the rest of the Mu'tazila, lest they think he retracted from their doctrine, as he alluded in the Divine Things, or that the claimant of self-evidence is other than him, even if not mentioned in this book, and God knows best. His statement: 'i.e., accepting'—indicates that the counter is not in its apparent form, because it is the adducing of evidence contrary to what the opponent adduced, and the opponent's claim is that the judgment is necessary; and in this sense his statement later: 'and they counter them', so do not be heedless.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth doubt against necessary sciences: that different schools claim self-evidence for conflicting propositions, which would create confusion. To illustrate, it gives two examples. First, the Mu'tazila claim that 'truthful beneficial speech is good and harmful lying is bad' is self-evident, but Ash'aris and philosophers deny this and classify it as a well-known (mashhurat) proposition, not a primary self-evident one. Second, the Mu'tazila claim that the servant is the independent creator of his voluntary acts is self-evident, but Ash'aris and philosophers counter with a different necessity: every act needs a preponderator from outside to avoid infinite regress. The commentary then clarifies key points: (1) The term 'good' in this debate means a basis for reward, not harmony with purpose; intellect does not judge reward and punishment. (2) The claimant of self-evidence for the second example is Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, but he does not actually hold that position; he used it as a tactic. (3) The philosophers actually agree with Abu al-Husayn's real view, so their opposition here is only apparent. The passage shows that disagreement about supposed self-evident propositions does not undermine genuine necessary knowledge, because the disputed cases are not truly self-evident.

Key Terms
شبهة سادسة
بديهة
مشهورات
حسن وقبح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that because there is disagreement about a proposition, it cannot be self-evident. This passage shows that the disagreement itself is a problem, but the solution is that the disputed examples are not truly self-evident.
Correction: The passage argues that the examples are not self-evident but well-known, so the doubt does not affect genuine self-evident propositions.

Checkpoint
How do the Ash'aris and philosophers classify the Mu'tazilite proposition about good and bad?
A) As a self-evident primary proposition.
B) As a speculative proposition.
C) As a well-known proposition (mashhurat) that may be false.
D) As a revealed truth.

Key Takeaway
The sixth doubt is neutralized by showing that conflicting claims of self-evidence involve propositions that are not truly self-evident but well-known (mashhurat).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What 'self-evident' moral belief do you hold most firmly? Have you ever considered that it might be a 'well-known' (mashhūr) convention rather than an absolute rational truth? This week, consciously examine one such belief — ask yourself: if the Ash'aris are right that even the goodness of truthfulness is not self-evident to reason, where does your certainty really come from?
?
When a fellow Muslim disagrees with you on a moral issue you consider obvious, how do you react? The lesson shows that disagreement does not necessarily destroy certainty; the proposition may not be genuinely self-evident. Will you commit to engaging in calm epistemological analysis next time you face such a disagreement, rather than dismissing the other person?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed that reason alone dictates moral values, while the Ash'aris argued that these are conventional. Which camp do you instinctively lean toward in your daily decision-making? Are you willing to let revelation challenge your 'obvious' moral intuitions, even if it feels uncomfortable? Decide on one specific moral conviction you will re-evaluate today.

The Second Example: The Servant as Independent Creator and the Preponderator Refutation
Al-Jurjani presents the second example of disputed self-evidence concerning human free will and the preponderator response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second example of disputed self-evidence—the Mu'tazilite claim that the servant is the independent creator of his acts—and how the Ash'aris and philosophers counter it with the necessity of a preponderator from outside.

Core Idea
The alleged self-evidence of human free will is countered by the necessary truth that every act requires a preponderator, preventing infinite regress.

The second example in al-Jurjani's discussion is the Mu'tazilite claim that a human being independently creates his voluntary acts, and some claim this is self-evident. The Ash'aris and philosophers deny this and counter with another necessary truth: every act must have a preponderator from outside, otherwise infinite regress would follow. This lesson examines how this counter-argument shows that the original claim is not self-evident but rather leads to a contradiction, and clarifies the background of the claimant Abu al-Husayn al-Basri.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation for the second example, noting the two opposing claims.
Pay attention to the structure: Mu'tazilite claim, then Ash'ari/philosopher response.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite claim
They assert the servant is independent creator of his voluntary acts and some claim it is self-evident.
This is the proposition in dispute.
3
Understand the counter-argument
The Ash'aris and philosophers present a different necessity: every act needs a preponderator from outside to avoid infinite regress.
This uses the same claim of necessity to refute the original.
4
Reflect on the resolution
The disagreement shows that the proposition is not self-evident, as it is disputed and can be argued against.
5
Note the commentary
The gloss clarifies that the claimant (Abu al-Husayn) might have used this claim tactically.

This passage presents the sixth doubt against necessary sciences: that different schools claim self-evidence for conflicting propositions, which would create confusion. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Etymology of 'Sophist' and 'Philosopher'
Explains the Greek origins of sophist and philosopher.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Greek origins of the terms sophist and philosopher and how they were Arabized.

Core Idea
Sophist means science of error; philosopher means lover of wisdom.

The terms 'sophist' and 'philosopher' come from Greek and reflect opposite meanings. This lesson uncovers their etymological roots, showing that sophistry is linked to error while philosophy is linked to love of wisdom. Understanding this helps clarify why sophistry is rejected in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the full Arabic passage and its literal English translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the author's remark about rational people not adopting sophistry.
2
Analyze the Greek etymology
Break down the components: 'sufa' = science, 'ista' = error → 'sufasta' = science of error; 'fila' = lover, 'fus' (implied) = wisdom → 'filusuf' = lover of wisdom.
The passage explicitly explains both terms.
3
Reflect on the connotation
Consider why calling sophistry 'science of error' is a strong criticism, and how philosophy is elevated as love of wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل: ليس يمكن أن يكون في العالم قوم عقلاء ينتحلون هذا المذهب، بل كل غالط سوفسطائي في موضع غلطة، فإن سوفا بلغة اليونانيين اسم للعلم، واسطاً اسم للغلط فسوفسطا معناه علم الغلط، كما أن فيلا بلغتهم اسم المحب، وفيلسوف معناه محب العلم، ثم عرب هذان اللفظان واشتق منهما السفسطة والفلسفة
And it is said: It is not possible that there are rational people in the world who adopt this doctrine, rather every person who makes an error is a sophist in the place of his error. For 'sufa' in the language of the Greeks is the name for science, and 'ista' is the name for error, so 'sufasta' means science of error. Just as 'fila' in their language is the name for lover, and 'filusuf' means lover of science. Then these two terms were Arabized and from them were derived 'safsata' and 'falsafa'.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage provides the etymology of the terms 'sophist' (sufasta) and 'philosopher' (filusuf) from Greek. It first notes that it is unlikely that rational people actually adopt the sophist doctrine; rather, anyone who errs is a sophist in that instance. Then it explains that in Greek, 'sufa' means science and 'ista' means error, so 'sufasta' means science of error. Similarly, 'fila' means lover, so 'filusuf' means lover of science (wisdom). These two terms were Arabized into 'safsata' (sophistry) and 'falsafa' (philosophy). This clarifies the linguistic origin and the negative connotation of sophistry.

Key Terms
سوفسطا
فيلسوف
السفسطة
الفلسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sophistry and philosophy are related disciplines.
Correction: The etymology shows they are opposites: sophistry is the science of error, while philosophy is the love of wisdom.

Checkpoint
What does 'sufasta' mean in Greek according to the passage?
A) Lover of science
B) Science of error
C) Science of truth
D) Error of science

Key Takeaway
The Greek roots reveal that sophistry is the science of error, while philosophy is the love of wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you catch yourself in an error of reasoning, do you humbly correct it, or do you defend it like a sophist? What would it take for you to become a true lover of wisdom instead?
?
Examine your inner circle: do you surround yourself with people who love truth or those who are content with error? How will you change this starting today?
?
What is one deep-seated belief you hold that you have never critically examined? Are you willing to test it against the truth, even if it means letting go of something comfortable?

Objection: Is the Definition of Nazar Mere Enumeration?
The objection and response regarding definition vs. enumeration of nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objector claims that al-Qadi's definition of nazar is mere enumeration and how the author defends it as a valid definition by characteristic (rasm).

Core Idea
A definition can be by characteristic (rasm) even if it mentions divisions, capturing a distinguishing feature.

This lesson addresses a subtle methodological objection in kalam: is the definition of nazar (speculative thought) a true definition or just an enumeration of its types? The objector argues that it is mere enumeration, but the author responds that it is a definition by characteristic (rasm) that explains the concept through its distinguishing property. Understanding this debate is key to grasping how kalam scholars define their terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Identify the objector's claim that the definition is ta'did (enumeration) not tahdid (definition). Notice the two premises: minor (the definition lists two divisions) and major (enumeration is not definition).
The objector uses a syllogistic structure.
2
Read the author's response
Note that the author denies the minor premise, arguing that it is an explanation of the concept by its distinctive characteristic, not a mere listing.
The response is based on the idea of rasm.
3
Understand the concept of rasm
A definition by description (rasm) uses a distinctive property (khaassah) to define, even if that property involves division into kinds.
The author gives an example: 'thought by which one of the two matters is sought' captures the common element.
4
Reflect on the subtlety
The passage warns that this point is often misunderstood, so pay attention to how the common element between the two divisions is taken as the defining feature.
This is a key insight for kalam methodology.
