So reflect, and do not pay attention to the doubts that arose inadvertently from his saying: (The definition etc.) Formulation of the question: That which al-Qadi mentioned in defining nazar is an enumeration of its divisions. And nothing of enumeration is definition. As for the minor premise: that which seeks knowledge and that which seeks opinion are two divisions falling under nazar. As for the major: enumeration is an explanation of the divisions, and definition is an explanation of the concept of the thing as such. The summary of the answer: We do not concede that it is an enumeration of its divisions; rather it is an explanation of its concept in terms of its distinctive characteristic, namely its division into them. However, since the characteristic of its concept alone is not true of its individuals, he took that characteristic in a way that it becomes true of its individuals by taking the common element between the two divisions and returning it to what is the cause of its division into them. So it was said: 'Thought by which one of the two matters, whichever it may be, is sought' is a definition by description (rasm). So reflect, for it is something hidden from peoples and feet have slipped therein.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents the first major objection to the definition of 'nazar' (speculative thought) and the response to it. The objector claims that al-Qadi's definition is actually an enumeration (ta'did) of the kinds of nazar (knowledge-seeking and opinion-seeking) rather than a true definition (tahdid) which should explain the concept itself. The author responds that it is a definition by characteristic (rasm) that specifies the distinctive feature of nazar, namely that it is divided into these two kinds. The definition is not a mere enumeration because it captures the common element between the two kinds and explains the concept in terms of its distinguishing property. This is a subtle point that scholars sometimes miss.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a definition cannot refer to the division of a concept; any mention of types makes it an enumeration rather than a definition.
Correction: A definition by description (rasm) can validly use a distinctive property that involves division into kinds, as long as it captures a common element that applies to all individuals.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is too broad and includes non-nazar things.
B) Because it merely enumerates the divisions of nazar rather than explaining its essence.
C) Because it uses the word 'or' which creates ambiguity.
D) Because it mentions both knowledge and opinion.

Key Takeaway
A definition by characteristic (rasm) is valid even if it alludes to the division of a concept, as long as it captures a distinguishing property that applies to all individuals of that concept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author warns that many have slipped on this subtle point between enumeration and definition by characteristic. Have you ever been overconfident in understanding a theological concept, only to later realize you missed its essence? How will you humble yourself to avoid such slips? Resolve to study each term with the depth it demands.
?
When you encounter a definition that lists parts or divisions, do you dismiss it as incomplete? Could your insistence on a strict 'hadd' definition blind you to valid 'rasm' definitions that capture the essence? Commit to evaluating definitions on their explanatory power, not just their form.
?
The objector's error stemmed from a rigid view of definition. Is there an area of your faith where you hold a rigid, narrow understanding of a concept (e.g., tawheed, iman) that might miss the richness intended by scholars? Pray for the humility to accept definitions that expand your understanding.

The Objection of Non-Inclusiveness and Its Resolution
The author addresses the objection that the characteristic is not inclusive and provides a response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that the characteristic in a definition by description is not comprehensive is refuted by distinguishing between actual and potential properties.

Core Idea
A characteristic in a definition can be potential, not just actual.

This passage tackles a second critical objection: the characteristic used to define nazar—its division into knowledge-seeking and opinion-seeking—seems not to apply to all instances. The response clarifies that the definition concerns the nature of nazar as movement in intelligibles, which potentially yields knowledge or opinion. This nuanced understanding resolves the objection and reinforces the validity of the definition by description.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Read the opening sentences where the objector claims the characteristic is not inclusive and the initial reply that the definition by description does not require comprehensiveness.
Notice that the objector argues that some forms of reasoning do not seek knowledge or opinion.
2
Understand the key distinction
The response shifts the focus from actual seeking to the potential of seeking—thought (fkr) is movement in intelligibles, which by its nature can lead to knowledge or opinion.
This is the core of the refutation.
3
Examine the analogy with defining animal
The passage compares to defining animal as 'capable of speech,' which applies to all animals even though not all speak, because it's about potential.
This illustrates how a potential property can serve as a comprehensive characteristic.
4
Conclude with the reaffirmation
The passage ends by reiterating that al-Qadi's definition is a definition by description (rasm), and the division into two kinds is a distinguishing characteristic of nazar.
This solidifies the response to the objection.

Commentary
This passage deals with a second objection: that the characteristic (khassa) used in the definition (the division into knowledge-seeking and opinion-seeking) is not inclusive of all individuals of nazar. The passage concludes by reaffirming that al-Qadi's definition is a definition by characteristic (rasm), and the division into two kinds is a distinguishing property of nazar.

The Objection of 'Aw' as Hesitation
Objection and response about 'aw' in definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that 'aw' (or) in definitions indicates hesitation and ambiguity, and the response that it actually indicates division (taqsim), preserving clarity.

Core Idea
The word 'or' in definitions indicates division, not hesitation.

This lesson introduces a key objection to the definition of nazar: the word 'aw' (or) seems to suggest hesitation between knowledge and opinion, which would contradict the purpose of a clear definition. We will see how the author answers this by reinterpreting 'aw' as indicating division (taqsim), meaning 'whichever of the two kinds.'

Guided Path
1
Read the objection against 'aw'
Read the passage where the objector claims that 'aw' is for hesitation, causing ambiguity that conflicts with definition (tahdid).
Notice the objector's reasoning: hesitation → ambiguity → contradiction of definition's goal of clarity.
2
Identify the response
Find the answer: 'aw' is not for hesitation but for division (taqsim). Understand the implications: the definition remains unambiguous because it means 'whichever of the two kinds'.
3
Reflect on the categories
Consider how the definition of nazar as 'thought seeking either knowledge or opinion' uses 'aw' to present a division, not a choice between alternatives.
This preserves the definition's comprehensiveness without ambiguity.

His saying: (And this question may be formulated, etc.) It is understood from this expression that the question is in reality one, and the difference is only in expression. But it is not so, because the upshot of the first is that 'or' is for division, and division contradicts definition, and the upshot of the second is that 'or' is for hesitation, and it contradicts definition. Yes, the origin of both questions is one, namely the occurrence of the word 'or' in the definition. The most that can be said is that the third question is that the word 'or' contradicts definition, and its contradiction has been established by that expression and by this expression. His saying: ('Or' is for hesitation because it is placed for one of two matters without specification.) His saying: (Its summary, etc.) Since the expression of the text gives the impression that both divisions are included in the defined, thus making it an enumeration of its divisions not a definition, he pointed to its refutation by saying that the intent is that the defined has two different divisions in reality, the definition of one of them is this, and the definition of the other is that. Their saying 'that which is such that it is sought by it' etc. is interpreted to mean that its nature is that it is movement in intelligibles to obtain the principles of the sought, and this is true of every individual of its individuals that it is a movement, just as the phrase 'possible for truth and falsehood' applies to our saying 'the sky is above us' and its like, which are known with certainty that one of the two sides occurs. But the possibility of such consideration in the definition of animal, with what was mentioned, is a place of investigation. Unless one commits to its validity upon this orientation, and the distance of the validity of this orientation is not hidden: that it removes the definition from the obvious, and that his saying 'or predominant opinion' becomes superfluous then, with no significant benefit. And the interpretation as free choice in expression is rejected in the context of definition, so reflect. His saying: (That which al-Qadi mentioned in defining nazar) By 'definition' (tahdid) in the terminology of most theologians is meant the comprehensive and exclusive definition, and here it is so, so the use of 'tahdid' does not contradict it being a definition by description (rasm). His saying: (And the division into them is a characteristic) It is said: Then it is a definition by the more specific (al-akhass), since it is not true of any individual by which only one of them is sought. In summary, the definiendum must be true of all individuals of the defined, and that is not the case for the division if by it is meant the divided. And it is answered that the definiendum is the equivalent one (al-musawi), but it is objected that it is a definition by the negative (al-anfa) because it is a definition that revolves around two matters, depending on knowledge of the two specific matters each of which is more hidden. And it is answered that its being more hidden is in terms of its essence, not in terms of its being distinguished in general.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents the third question concerning the use of the word 'aw' (or) in the definition of nazar. The objector argues that 'aw' indicates hesitation (taraddud) which creates ambiguity, contradicting the purpose of definition (tahdid) which is clarification. The answer is that 'aw' here is used for division (taqsim), meaning 'whichever of the two kinds,' so the definition remains clear: nazar is thought that seeks either knowledge or opinion. The author also discusses related issues: whether the definition is a definition by the more specific (al-akhass), and whether it is a definition by the negative (al-anfa) because it depends on knowledge of two specific matters. The resolution involves careful distinctions about the nature of the definition and the understanding of 'aw'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'or' in definitions always indicates hesitation or doubt about the defined.
Correction: In kalam definitions, 'or' can be used for division (taqsim), presenting two mutually exclusive kinds without ambiguity, thereby preserving the definition's clarity.

Why does the objector argue that the word 'aw' (or) in the definition of nazar is problematic?

Key Takeaway
The word 'aw' in the definition of nazar is for division, not hesitation, thus the definition remains clear and unambiguous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used the word 'or' to avoid taking a clear position on a matter of faith? How does this lesson's insistence on 'division' rather than 'hesitation' challenge you to be more decisive in your convictions?
?
When you explain Islamic concepts, do you aim for the same level of precision as the scholars here, or do you sometimes rely on vague language? What would it cost you to define the terms of your faith with the clarity of taqsim?
?
Consider a specific belief you hold that you might have expressed with 'or' as hesitation. Can you now reframe it as a division (taqsim) that clarifies rather than obfuscates? What step will you take this week to make that reframing real in your speech or teaching?

Unity of the Question and Division of the Defined
Clarification that the question is one and the definition divides the defined.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the second expression of the objection is not a separate question but a refinement, and that the definition does not include both divisions but rather each division is defined separately.

Core Idea
The definition divides the defined into two distinct kinds, not including both at once.

In this lesson, we explore a nuance: the expression 'aw' might give the impression that the definition includes both divisions, making it an enumeration rather than a definition. The author clarifies that the defined has two different kinds in reality, and the definition is for each kind separately. We will also see how this relates to the unity of the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the clarification on the question's unity
Read the part where the author explains that the two expressions of the objection are essentially the same question, just phrased differently.
Both objections focus on 'aw' contradicting definition, but one emphasizes division, the other hesitation.
2
Understand the correction about inclusion
The original text might imply that both divisions are included in the defined. The author corrects this: the intent is that the defined has two different divisions, each defined separately.
3
Reflect on the implication for the definition
Consider why this distinction matters: a definition must be true of each individual of the defined; if both divisions were included, the definition would not be specific.
This connects to the broader discussion of definitional criteria in kalam.

التوجيه أنه إخراج للتعريف عن المتبادر على أن قوله : أو غلبة ظن يكون مستدركاً حينئذ لا فائدة له يعتد بها، والحمل على التخيير في التعبير مدفوع في مقام التعريف فتأمل. قوله : (الذي ذكره القاضي في تحديد And this third question may be formulated (in this place and other definitions that involve hesitation (taraddud) in another expression, so it is said: (The word 'or' is for hesitation, and that is) i.e., hesitation (which causes ambiguity, so it contradicts definition (tahdid) which aims at clarity. The answer: Denying that (i.e., that 'or' in the definitions in which it is mentioned is for hesitation; rather it is for division (taqsim), i.e., whichever of the two divisions) mentioned in this definition, it is of the defined. The summary is that the meaning of 'or' here is 'any kind from...' His saying: (And this question may be formulated, etc.) It is understood from this expression that the question is in reality one, and the difference is only in expression. But it is not so, because the upshot of the first is that 'or' is for division, and division contradicts definition, and the upshot of the second is that 'or' is for hesitation, and it contradicts definition. Yes, the origin of both questions is one, namely the occurrence of the word 'or' in the definition. The most that can be said is that the third question is that the word 'or' contradicts definition, and its contradiction has been established by that expression and by this expression. His saying: ('Or' is for hesitation because it is placed for one of two matters without specification.) His saying: (Its summary, etc.) Since the expression of the text gives the impression that both divisions are included in the defined, thus making it an enumeration of its divisions not a definition, he pointed to its refutation by saying that the intent is that the defined has two different divisions in reality, the definition of one of them is this, and the definition of the other is that. Their saying 'that which is such that it is sought by it' etc. is interpreted to mean that its nature is that it is movement in intelligibles to obtain the principles of the sought, and this is true of every individual of its individuals that it is a movement, just as the phrase 'possible for truth and falsehood' applies to our saying 'the sky is above us' and its like, which are known with certainty that one of the two sides occurs. But the possibility of such consideration in the definition of animal, with what was mentioned, is a place of investigation. Unless one commits to its validity upon this orientation, and the distance of the validity of this orientation is not hidden: that it removes the definition from the obvious, and that his saying 'or predominant opinion' becomes superfluous then, with no significant benefit. And the interpretation as free choice in expression is rejected in the context of definition, so reflect. His saying: (That which al-Qadi mentioned in defining nazar) By 'definition' (tahdid) in the terminology of most theologians is meant the comprehensive and exclusive definition, and here it is so, so the use of 'tahdid' does not contradict it being a definition by description (rasm). His saying: (And the division into them is a characteristic) It is said: Then it is a definition by the more specific (al-akhass), since it is not true of any individual by which only one of them is sought. In summary, the definiendum must be true of all individuals of the defined, and that is not the case for the division if by it is meant the divided. And it is answered that the definiendum is the equivalent one (al-musawi), but it is objected that it is a definition by the negative (al-anfa) because it is a definition that revolves around two matters, depending on knowledge of the two specific matters each of which is more hidden. And it is answered that its being more hidden is in terms of its essence, not in terms of its being distinguished in general.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents the third question concerning the use of the word 'aw' (or) in the definition of nazar. The objector argues that 'aw' indicates hesitation (taraddud) which creates ambiguity, contradicting the purpose of definition (tahdid) which is clarification. The answer is that 'aw' here is used for division (taqsim), meaning 'whichever of the two kinds,' so the definition remains clear: nazar is thought that seeks either knowledge or opinion. The author also discusses related issues: whether the definition is a definition by the more specific (al-akhass), and whether it is a definition by the negative (al-anfa) because it depends on knowledge of two specific matters. The resolution involves careful distinctions about the nature of the definition and the understanding of 'aw'.

التقسيم
المحدود
الترديد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition including 'or' must include both alternatives in the defined, making it an enumeration.
Correction: The definition is actually for each division separately; 'or' indicates a division of the defined into two kinds, not a combination.

A) Both divisions are indeed included.
B) The definition is for each division separately.
C) The division is only apparent.
D) The defined has no divisions.

Key Takeaway
The definition uses 'or' to divide the defined into two real kinds, each defined separately, not to include both in one definition.

?

How might that assumption lead to confusion in understanding the nature of nazar (reflection) and its role in acquiring knowledge? How does this precision in logical thinking challenge your own tendency to accept vague or inclusive statements about matters of faith? Ask yourself: Do I settle for definitions that 'sort of' cover everything, or do I seek clarity that distinguishes truth from falsehood?

Objections of al-Akhass and al-Anfa in Definition
Objections and responses regarding definitional criteria.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections that the definition might be by the more specific (al-akhass) or by the negative (al-anfa), and the responses defending the definition's validity.

Core Idea
Definition by division can avoid being too specific or negative.

This lesson addresses further technical criticisms: some argue that the definition is a definition by the more specific (al-akhass) because it only applies to one division, or that it is a definition by the negative (al-anfa) because it depends on knowing two specific matters. We will see how the author responds to these challenges, ultimately defending the definition as valid.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection of al-akhass
The passage explains that if the definition is for only one division, it might not apply to all individuals of the defined, making it a definition by the more specific (al-akhass).
A proper definition must be true of every individual of the defined.
2
Read the response to al-akhass
The answer states that the definiendum is the equivalent one (al-musawi) – meaning the definition covers exactly those individuals that fall under the division.
3
Read the objection of al-anfa
Another objection: the definition is by the negative (al-anfa) because it requires knowing two specific matters that are more hidden.
A definition should not rely on something more obscure.
4
Read the response to al-anfa
The response distinguishes: the hiddenness is in terms of essence, not in terms of being distinguished in general.
Thus, the definition remains valid.

Distinguishing Division of the Defined from Division of the Definition
Sanusi addresses the objection and distinguishes division of the defined from division of the definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between a division of the defined and a division of the definition, and why defining something by its divisions is not logically circular.

Core Idea
Knowing whether a division belongs to the defined or the definition resolves objections about circularity.

This passage tackles a common logical concern: if a definition uses divisions, does it define the thing by its own parts? Sanusi clarifies that the division belongs to the defined, not the definition, using the example of 'body' to show that listing comprehensive features is different from listing alternative specifications.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and translation to grasp the initial objection and the author's response.
The objection claims defining by divisions is circular; the answer distinguishes types of division.
2
Identify the two examples
Notice the two ways to define 'body': one divides the defined (composed of atoms), the other divides the definition (composed of atoms OR has dimensions). Understand which is which.
3
Apply to the current definition
See how the author applies this to the definition of thought (al-fikr) and seeking (al-talab). The division is of the defined because 'seeking' is a comprehensive matter.
4
Understand the genus-species terminology
Learn that the ancients called the common element 'genus' and the distinguishing element 'species'. Here, al-fikr is genus, and the seeking of knowledge or opinion is the species.
This clarifies why the definition is considered a descriptive definition (rasm) according to the ancients.
5
Reflect on the logical significance
Consider how this distinction prevents the fallacy of circular definition. The divisions specify the defined, not the definition itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال: يلزم تعريف الشيء بأقسامه ويجاب بأن القسم ذات الأمرين والمعرف هو المفهوم. قوله: (فهو من المحدود) يعني أنه لتقسيم المحدود لا لتقسيم الحد والفرق أن الحد إذا اشتمل على أمر شامل فذا تقسيم المحدود، كان يقال: الجسم ما تركب من جوهرين أو أكثر، بخلاف أن يقال: ما تركب من جوهرين أو ماله طول وعرض وعمق، فإنه لتقسيم الحد وما نحن فيه من الأول، لأن الطلب يشملهما. قوله: (فلا يكون منافياً إلخ) لشمولها بهذا المعنى للمعقولات والمتخيلات، فلا يرد ما ذكر في شرح المقاصد. قوله: (فهو جنس للنظر والباقي فصل) قد سبق الإشارة إلى أن القدماء يسمون ما به الاشتراك مطلقاً جنساً، وما به الامتياز فصلاً، وإطلاق الجنس على الفكر بالمعنى المتعارف بين المتأخرين، كما دل عليه السياق وأما إطلاق الفصل على الباقي، فلعله على اصطلاح وعلى هذا لا ينافي إطلاق الجنس والفصل هاهنا تصريحه فيما سبق، بكون هذا التعريف رسمياً للقدماء.
And it may be said: Defining a thing by its divisions is necessary, and the answer is that the division is of the two things and the definer is the concept. His statement: 'It is from the defined' means that it is a division of the defined, not a division of the definition. The difference is that when the definition includes a comprehensive matter, that is a division of the defined, as is said: 'The body is that which is composed of two or more atoms,' unlike saying: 'That which is composed of two atoms or has length, width, and depth,' for that would be a division of the definition. Our case is the first, because the seeking (al-talab) encompasses both (knowledge and opinion). His statement: 'So it is not contradictory, etc.' because it includes in this meaning conceptual and imaginative matters, so it does not contradict what was mentioned in Sharh al-Maqasid. His statement: 'So it (al-fikr) is a genus for nazar and the remainder is a species' — it was previously indicated that the ancients call that by which sharing occurs, absolutely, 'genus', and that by which distinction occurs, 'species'. And the application of 'genus' to al-fikr in the conventional sense of the later scholars, as the context indicates, and as for applying 'species' to the remainder (the seeking of knowledge or opinion), it is perhaps according to terminology. And on this, it does not contradict his statement in what preceded, that this definition is a descriptive definition (rasm) according to the ancients.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage handles an objection that the definition defines a thing by its divisions. The answer explains that the definition is a division of the defined, not of the definition itself. The example of 'body' clarifies the difference: 'body is composed of two or more atoms' divides the defined (body), whereas listing alternative specifications like 'composed of two atoms or has dimensions' divides the definition. Our case is like the first, because the common element 'seeking' encompasses both knowledge and opinion. Then the author explains that the common element (al-fikr) serves as genus, and the specifying element (seeking knowledge or opinion) serves as species. This is in line with the terminology of the ancients, and it does not contradict that the definition is a descriptive definition (rasm).

Key Terms
الجنس
الفصل
الرسم
الحد
المحدود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any definition that lists divisions is circular because it defines a thing by its own parts.
Correction: A division of the defined uses a comprehensive property common to all individuals, which does not define the thing by its parts but by a shared characteristic. A division of the definition lists alternative specifications, which would be circular, but that is not what is happening here.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes a division of the defined from a division of the definition?
A) A division of the defined includes a comprehensive matter and a single specification
B) A division of the definition lists alternative properties
C) Both are the same
D) The division of the defined is used in kalam

Key Takeaway
A definition that uses a single comprehensive property to cover all instances is a division of the defined, not the definition, thus avoiding circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a concept you often discuss (e.g., 'tawakkul', 'sincerity'). When you define it, do you tend to list examples (like 'not worrying, planning, trusting') or do you find a single comprehensive property that covers all instances? Which method does Sanusi advocate here? Examine your own tendency—are you prone to circular or fragmented definitions? Commit to refining your definition based on this lesson.
?
Consider the distinction between division of the defined and division of the definition. In your own learning of Islamic sciences, have you ever felt that a definition was 'circular' or unhelpful? After this lesson, can you analyze whether it was actually a division of the definition? How does this skill protect you from confusion in understanding advanced texts? Set a goal to apply this analysis to the next definition you encounter.

Genus and Species in the Definition of Nazar
Sanusi discusses genus and species in definition, refutes Al-Amidi, clarifies tahsiliyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the roles of genus and species in the definition of nazar, the refutation of Al-Amidi's view, and the meaning of 'tahsiliyya' in this context.

Core Idea
Genus gives the root quiddity; species perfects it — both are essential for definition.

Sanusi continues his analysis of the definition of nazar by examining the roles of genus and species. He argues that the genus is necessary to indicate the foundational essence, while the species distinguishes it from other acts. He also refutes Al-Amidi's alternative interpretation and clarifies that 'tahsiliyya' here means actualization, not the technical sense of removing ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the role of genus and species
The author states that 'al-fikr' is the genus for nazar, and the remainder is the species that distinguishes it. Read the first part to see why both are needed: genus indicates the root quiddity, species perfects and distinguishes.
The author uses the example: if you define nazar as 'that by which knowledge is sought', the root quiddity is not understood.
2
Examine Al-Amidi's objection
Al-Amidi claimed that the author's phrase 'nazar is fikr' is not a part of the definition but a statement of synonymy. The author refutes this as an 'obvious artifice' because the expression clearly suggests definition, not synonymy.
Notice how the author relies on the apparent meaning of the words.
3
Clarify the meaning of 'tahsiliyya'
The author explains that 'tahsiliyya' (perfection) here is lexical, meaning actualization in reality, not the technical sense of removing ambiguity. This distinction is crucial for understanding how genus and species function.
Also note the conclusion about applying 'genus' and 'species' absolutely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهو بالمعنى الذي ذكرناه جنس للنظر لا مرادف له على ما هو المتعارف. والباقي من الحد فصل له يميزه عن سائر الحركات التخيلية. ولا يقال: أن الفصل كاف في التمييز والجنس مستغن عنه في الحد، كيف والجنس هو الذي يدل على أصل الماهية والفصل يحصلها ويميزها، ألا ترى أنك إذا قلت: النظر هو الذي يطلب به علم أو ظن، لم يفهم منه أصل ماهية النظر بل ربما أوهم شموله لغير النظر مما له مدخل في ذلك الطلب. قال الآمدي: لم يذكره جزاءً من التعريف بل قال: النظر هو الفكر بياناً لاتحاد مدلولهما، وما بعده الحد لهما. وفيه تمحل لا يخفى لأن بيان الترادف واتحاد المدلول في مقام التحديد بعبارة ظاهرة في خلافه بعيد جداً، وإنما كانت ظاهرة في خلاف بيان ماذا هو هو. قوله: (فهو جنس للنظر والباقي فصل) له بناء على ما تقرر من أن المفهومات الاصطلاحية ماهيات اعتبارية فما اعتبر داخلاً في مفهوماتها فهو ذاتي لها. قوله: (والفصل يحصلها إلخ) التحصيل بالمعنى اللغوي أي جعلها حاصلة متحققة في نفس الأمر لا بالمعنى الاصطلاحي، أعني إزالة إبهام الجنس وجعله مطابقاً لتمام ماهية النوع، فإنها تنسب إلى الجنس لا الماهية النوعية. ثم القول: بكون الباقي فصلاً بمعنى المميز الذاتي لا ينافي ما ذكره سابقاً من أن هذا التعريف رسمي، وأن الانقسام خاصة له لأن ذلك مبني على أن يكون ما ذكره تعريفاً لمطلق النظر، ولا شك أن الانقسام إلى الأقسام ليس داخلاً في المقسم، وهذا مبني على أن يكون ما ذكره تعريفاً لقسميه، فالنظر العلمي الفكر الذي يطلب به العلم والنظر الظني الفكر الذي يطلب به الظن، وكل واحد من المشترك والمميز داخل في مفهوميهما. وحمل الجنس على الذاتي والفصل على المميز مطلقاً، مما لا يقبله الطبع السليم سيما إذا لوحظ قوله: والفصل يحصلها.
So it (al-fikr) in the sense we mentioned is a genus for nazar, not a synonym according to common usage. And the remainder of the definition is a species that distinguishes it from other imaginative movements. And it is not said that the species is sufficient for distinction and the genus is dispensable in the definition, because the genus indicates the root of the quiddity, and the species perfects and distinguishes it. Do you not see that if you say: 'nazar is that by which knowledge or opinion is sought,' the root quiddity of nazar is not understood, and perhaps it suggests inclusion of other than nazar that has a part in that seeking. Al-Amidi said: He did not mention it as a part of the definition, but said: 'nazar is fikr' to indicate the unity of their denotation, and what follows is the definition of them both. There is an obvious artifice in this, because indicating synonymity and unity of denotation in the context of definition with an expression apparently contradicting that is far-fetched. His statement: 'So it is a genus for nazar and the remainder is a species' is based on the established principle that conventional concepts are considered quiddities, so what is considered to be internal to their concepts is essential to them. His statement: 'and the species perfects them, etc.' — 'perfection' here is lexically, i.e., making them actualized in reality, not in the technical sense of removing the ambiguity of the genus and making it correspond to the complete quiddity of the species, because that is attributed to the genus, not to the specific quiddity. Then saying that the remainder is a species in the sense of a distinctive essential does not contradict his earlier statement that this definition is a descriptive definition (rasm), and that the division is a property of it, because that is based on the assumption that what he mentioned is a definition of absolute nazar, and there is no doubt that division into parts is not internal to the divided. And this is based on the assumption that what he mentioned is a definition of its two parts, so speculative nazar is the thought by which knowledge is sought, and suppositional nazar is the thought by which opinion is sought, and each of the common and the distinctive is internal to their concepts. And applying 'genus' to the essential and 'species' to the distinctive absolutely is not accepted by sound nature, especially when considering his statement: 'and the species perfects them.'
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on genus and species. It argues that the genus is necessary to indicate the root quiddity, and the species to specify. It refutes Al-Amidi's view that the definition is merely a synonym indication, calling it an obvious artifice. It clarifies that 'perfection' here means actualization in reality, not the technical sense of removing ambiguity. The passage concludes that treating the common element as essential and the distinguishing element as species is problematic, and that the definition can be seen as a rasm (descriptive) when considering absolute nazar, but as a hadd (definition) when considering its two parts.

Key Terms
الجنس
الفصل
الرسم
الحد
الماهية
التحصيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The species alone is sufficient for definition because it distinguishes.
Correction: The genus is necessary because it provides the root quiddity; without it, the definition does not convey the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the genus not dispensable in the definition?
A) Because it is the only distinguishing element
B) Because it indicates the root of the quiddity
C) Because it is required by Al-Amidi
D) Because it is a convention

Key Takeaway
Genus and species are both essential for a proper definition: genus indicates the root quiddity, species distinguishes and perfects it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you define what you truly believe in, do you stop to identify the root quiddity of that belief, or do you only grasp at its distinguishing marks? How might your faith deepen if you sought the essence of your convictions rather than just their distinctive features?
?
The author warns against applying 'genus' and 'species' absolutely. In your own judgments about others, have you ever categorized a person based on a superficial distinguishing trait while ignoring their underlying humanity? How can you ensure you see the whole quiddity?
?
Sanusi rejects Al-Amidi's attempt to force a synonymy between nazar and fikr in the definition. Are there areas in your religious understanding where you have accepted a convenient interpretation that avoids the full truth? What would it cost you to embrace the clear, demanding truth instead?

Defending the Definition: Over-Inclusivity Objection
Sanusi answers the objection that the definition might include life, intellect, or other things besides nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection that the definition of nazar might inadvertently include life, intellect, or other entities, and how he handles an alternative interpretation by al-Amidi.

Core Idea
A definition's precision is saved by the concept of proximate causation.

Sanusi anticipates a potential flaw: the definition of nazar might appear to include things like life, intellect, or the soul. He responds by showing that the choice of words and the interpretation of the preposition 'ba' as proximate causation exclude those. He then critiques an alternative reading by al-Amidi, demonstrating his strict methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the core response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objection claims the definition might encompass other entities. Sanusi's immediate response points to the word 'iktisab' (acquisition) and the interpretation of the ba' as indicating proximate causation.
Focus on how the ba' restricts the meaning.
2
Understand 'al-iham' and 'wahm'
Sanusi uses the word 'al-iham' (suggestion) to indicate that the objection is based on false estimation (wahm), not a real flaw. Reflect on why he dismisses it as a mere 'suggestion'.
Wahm is a lower cognitive faculty; this shows the objection is not strong.
3
Analyze the critique of al-Amidi's interpretation
Sanusi discusses al-Amidi's alternative reading of the definition as merely a descriptive definition (rasm) using genus and property. He notes a deviation in the phrase 'the species perfects them' unless explained as an apposition. See how he refines the understanding.
This shows Sanusi's engagement with other scholars' views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: بل ربما أوهم شموله لغير النظر كالحياة والقوة العاقلة والنفس والدليل ووجه الدلالة وبالجملة ما له مدخل في الاكتساب، وأشار بلفظ الإيهام إلى كونه باطلاً من أحكام الوهم، لا إلى ضعفه واندفاعه بحمل الباء على السببية القريبة، فإن الفكر معد للعلم والظن وليس سبباً قريباً لهما. قوله: (بياناً لاتحاد مدلولهما) أي مفهومها فالآمدي حمل الفكر على المعنى المتعارف. بناء على أن المركب من الجنس والخاصة رسم كما سيأتي، لكن في قوله والفصل يحصلها بعض نبوة عن هذا التوجيه، إلا أن يجعل قوله ويميزها عطفاً تفسيراً له.
His statement: 'But perhaps it suggests inclusion of other than nazar, like life, intellect, the soul, the proof, and the aspect of indication, and in short whatever has input in acquisition.' And he gestured with the word 'suggestion' (al-iham) to indicate that it is false according to the judgments of wahm (estimation), not to its weakness and its repulsion by interpreting the ba' as proximate causation, for fikr is preparative for knowledge and opinion, not a proximate cause for them. His statement: 'As an indication of the unity of their denotation' i.e., of their concepts, so al-Amidi interpreted fikr in the conventional sense, based on the idea that the combination of genus and property is a descriptive definition (rasm), as will come, but in his statement 'the species perfects them' there is some deviation from this direction, unless his statement 'and distinguishes them' is made an explanatory apposition.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection that the definition might be too broad, including things like life, intellect, etc. The response explains that the word 'iktisab' (acquisition) and the interpretation of the ba' as proximate causation prevent that. The author then discusses Al-Amidi's interpretation of the definition as merely indicating synonymity, and critiques it, noting a deviation in the phrase 'the species perfects them' unless it is taken as an explanatory apposition.

Key Terms
الإيهام
الوهم
السببية القريبة
الاكتساب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the definition of nazar is carelessly worded and inadvertently includes life, intellect, etc.
Correction: The definition is precise: using 'ba' for proximate causation ensures that only nazar (as a proximate cause) is included, and other things are excluded because they are only preparatory.

Checkpoint
How does the author argue that the definition does not include life or intellect?
A) By restricting the ba' to proximate causation
B) By stating that only fikr is included
C) By excluding life from the definition
D) By saying that the definition is explicitly narrow

Key Takeaway
The objection that the definition includes extraneous entities is refuted by the precise interpretation of 'ba' as proximate causation, and al-Amidi's alternative is shown to be problematic unless properly understood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
In your own life, do you confuse what merely prepares the way (like resources or environment) with what actually causes outcomes? How might the distinction between preparative and proximate cause change how you assess your actions?
?
Sanusi critiques al-Amidi's wording with precision. Are you as meticulous with your own language—especially when defining core beliefs? What would it cost you to be less careful?

Jins and Fasl as Essential Parts of Nazar
The commentary explains how jins and fasl function as essential parts of the definition of nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how jins (genus) and fasl (differentia) serve as essential parts in the definition of nazar, and how the commentary clarifies the meaning of 'actualization' and reconciles with the descriptive nature of the definition.

Core Idea
Jins and fasl are essential because conventional concepts are conventional quiddities.

In this passage, the commentary unpacks the statement that fikr is the genus of nazar and the remainder is its differentia. It builds on the premise that conventional concepts are considered conventional quiddities, so what is included in their concept is essential. The explanation also clarifies the linguistic meaning of 'actualization' and reconciles with an earlier point that the definition is descriptive rather than essential.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus and differentia
Read the passage and note that it identifies fikr as the genus for nazar and the remaining parts as the differentia. Understand that this is based on the idea that conventional concepts are conventional quiddities, making these parts essential.
The phrase 'فهو جنس للنظر والباقي فصل له' sets the stage.
2
Understand the meaning of 'actualization'
The commentary explains that 'al-faslu yuhassiluha' means actualization in the linguistic sense—making the species real—not the technical sense of removing ambiguity. Reflect on why this distinction matters.
The commentary explicitly contrasts 'بالمعنى اللغوي' with 'بالمعنى الاصطلاحي'.
3
Reconcile with the descriptive definition
The passage addresses an apparent contradiction: earlier the definition was called 'rasmiy' (descriptive), but now it includes essential parts. The solution is that the definition can be applied to the two kinds of nazar—scientific and speculative—each having a common and distinguishing part.
This shows how the same definition can be viewed from different angles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (فهو جنس للنظر والباقي فصل له) بناء على ما تقرر من أن المفهومات الاصطلاحية ماهيات اعتبارية، فما اعتبر داخلاً في مفهوماتها فهو ذاتي لها. قوله: (والفصل يحصلها إلخ) التحصيل بالمعنى اللغوي أي جعلها حاصلة متحققة في نفس الأمر لا بالمعنى الاصطلاحي، أعني إزالة إبهام الجنس، وجعله مطابقاً لتمام ماهية النوع، فإنها تنسب إلى الجنس لا الماهية النوعية. ثم القول بكون الباقي فصلاً بمعنى المميز الذاتي لا ينافي ما ذكره سابقاً من أن هذا التعريف رسمي، وأن الانقسام خاصة له، لأن ذلك مبني على أن يكون ما ذكره تعريفاً لمطلق النظر، ولا شك أن الانقسام إلى الأقسام ليس داخلاً في المقسم، وهذا مبني على أن يكون ما ذكره تعريفاً لقسميه، فالنظر العلمي الفكر الذي يطلب به العلم والنظر الظني الفكر الذي يطلب به الظن، وكل واحد من المشترك والمميز داخل في مفهوميهما، وحمل الجنس على الذاتي والفصل على المميز مطلقاً مما لا يقبله الطبع السليم سيما إذا لوحظ قوله: والفصل يحصلها.
His statement: (So it is a genus for nazar and the remainder is a differentia for it) based on what has been established that conventional concepts are conventional quiddities, so what is considered part of their concepts is essential to them. His statement: (And the differentia actualizes them, etc.) Actualization in the linguistic sense, i.e., making them actual and realized in reality, not in the technical sense, namely removing the ambiguity of the genus and making it correspond to the complete quiddity of the species, for they are attributed to the genus, not the specific quiddity. Then the statement that the remainder is a differentia in the sense of the essential distinguisher does not contradict what he mentioned earlier that this definition is descriptive and that division is a property of it, because that is based on his statement being a definition of absolute nazar, and there is no doubt that division into parts is not included in the divided, and this is based on his statement being a definition of its two parts: scientific nazar is thought by which knowledge is sought, and speculative nazar is thought by which opinion is sought, and each of the common and distinguishing is included in their concepts. And applying the genus to the essential and the differentia to the distinguisher absolutely is not accepted by sound nature, especially when considering his statement: and the differentia actualizes them.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage explains the statement "فهو جنس للنظر والباقي فصل له" (so it [fikr] is the genus of nazar, and the rest is its differentia). It builds on the premise that conventional concepts (المفهومات الاصطلاحية) are considered conventional quiddities (ماهيات اعتبارية), so what is considered part of their concept is essential (ذاتي) to them. The word "ماهية" in the text refers to this. The phrase "والفصل يحصلها" (the differentia makes it actual) is taken in the linguistic sense of making something existent, not the technical sense of removing ambiguity. Then the author reconciles with the earlier statement that this definition is "رسمي" (descriptive) and that الانقسام (division) is a property of it. This is based on whether the definition is of nazar in general or of its two kinds (scientific and speculative). Each kind has a common part and a distinguishing part, and calling the genus "essential" and the differentia "distinguishing" absolutely (مطلقاً) is not accepted by sound nature, especially given the phrase "والفصل يحصلها".

Key Terms
جنس
فصل
ذاتي
ماهية
رسمي
اعتباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That jins and fasl are only technical terms without deeper significance.
Correction: The passage clarifies that they are essential (dhatiy) because the concepts involved are conventional quiddities; thus, jins and fasl are actual constituents of the definition.

Checkpoint
What does 'al-faslu yuhassiluha' mean according to the commentary?
A) It makes the species actual in the linguistic sense.
B) It removes the generality of the genus.
C) It distinguishes the species from others.
D) It is a purely technical term.

Key Takeaway
The jins and fasl in the definition of nazar are essential because conventional concepts are treated as quiddities, and the phrase 'al-faslu yuhassiluha' means linguistic actualization, not technical specification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you define core concepts in your faith or worldview, do you treat them as mere labels or as essential quiddities? What would change if you approached each definition as a real, essential part of your understanding?
?
The passage distinguishes between absolute nazar and its two types. In your own pursuit of truth, are you seeking certain knowledge (scientific nazar) or mere opinion (speculative nazar)? What is one step you can take today to move from opinion to certainty?
?
The commentator warns against applying 'jins' and 'fasl' absolutely without qualification. When have you applied a term too broadly or lazily, causing misunderstanding? Commit to one specific term you will use more precisely this week.

Derived Terms: Identity in Essence, Difference in Consideration
Commentator analyzes meaning of derived term and rejects composition objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a derived term and its source are identical in essence and differ only by consideration, and why the alternative view leads to a problematic composition.

Core Idea
A derived term and its source are one in essence, not composed.

The commentator addresses a debate about the meaning of derived terms. Some argue that a derived term implies composition because it includes both the source and a relation. This lesson explores why that view is flawed and presents the correct understanding: identity in essence, difference in consideration.

Guided Path
1
Read the original argument
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully to grasp the commentator's discussion.
2
Identify the objection
Notice the commentator's criticism: if a derived term meant 'something with the source', then the universal accident and the relation would enter into the differentia of real essences.
3
Understand the correct view
The verification is that the derived term and its source are identical in essence, differing only by consideration, as mentioned by al-Dawani.
4
Reflect on the implications
Realize that what people call the 'meaning of the derived term' is actually an expression of its necessary concomitant, not its real meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ومعنى المشتق إلخ فيه بحث لأنه لو كان معناه ذلك لزم دخول العرض العام أعني شيء، والنسبة في فصل الماهيات الحقيقية، والتحقيق أن المشتق والمشتق منه متحدان بالذات مختلفان بالاعتبار كما ذكره المحقق الدواني في حواشيه القديمة وإن ما قالوا : من معنى المشتق فهو تعبير عنه بلازمه.
His statement: 'And the meaning of the derived term...' etc. — there is investigation in it, because if its meaning were that, then the universal accident, namely 'thing', and the relation would enter into the differentia of real essences. The verification is that the derived term and that from which it is derived are identical in essence, differing by consideration, as mentioned by al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani in his old glosses, and what they said about the meaning of the derived term is an expression of its necessary concomitant.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage discusses the meaning of a derived term (mushtaq). The commentator argues against the claim that a derived term implies composition because its meaning includes 'something that has the thing from which it is derived'. If that were the case, the universal accident (al-‘ard al-‘amm) and the relation (nisba) would enter into the differentia (fasl) of real essences, which is problematic. The correct view, according to al-Dawani, is that the derived term and its source are identical in essence (muttaḥidān bi al-dhāt) and differ only by consideration (mukhtalifān bi al-i‘tibār). What is said about the meaning of the derived term is merely an expression of its necessary concomitant (lāzim).

Key Terms
المشتق
العرض العام
النسبة
فصل الماهيات الحقيقية
المشتق والمشتق منه متحدان بالذات
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A derived term implies real composition because it includes both the source and a relation.
Correction: The derived term and its source are identical in essence, differing only by consideration; the definition is just an expression of a necessary concomitant.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the relationship between a derived term and its source?
A) They are completely different in essence.
B) They are identical in essence but differ by consideration.
C) The derived term includes the source as a part.
D) They have no relation.

Key Takeaway
Derived terms and their sources are one in essence, differing only by consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you described yourself with a label (e.g., 'sinner', 'successful'). In light of this lesson, are you separating that label from your essence? How can you reconcile your identity with your actions, knowing they are identical in essence but differ by consideration? What would change if you truly believed that your attributes do not add to your essence but reflect it from a different perspective?
?
Consider your relationship with Allah: you have attributes like 'believer', 'servant', 'grateful', etc. Do you sometimes act as if these are external additions rather than expressions of your very essence? If you truly understood that these are one with your soul, how would your worship and daily life change? What step can you take today to align your consideration with your essence?

Definition Condition and Non-Necessity of Arrangement
Commentary on definition condition and arrangement necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why adding an indicator removes a definition from being a proper limit (hadd) and why arrangement between parts is not always necessary for composition.

Core Idea
Definition requires essential attributes; composition does not require arrangement.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies two subtle points from logical debates. First, a true definition consists solely of essential (dhāti) attributes; adding an indicator makes it not a proper definition. Second, while some argue that arrangement between parts is necessary, the commentator shows that composition can exist without arrangement. Let's examine the Arabic text and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the two separate statements introduced by 'قوله'.
The passage is dense; take your time with each phrase.
2
Identify the two claims
The passage makes two distinct points: (1) A definition (ḥadd) must be composed only of essential (dhāti) attributes. (2) Arrangement (tartīb) is not obligatory for composition; composition can exist without it.
The second point is a response to a potential objection.
3
Reflect on the importance
Consider why these distinctions matter for logical definitions. The first ensures definitions are not confused with descriptions. The second shows that composition does not imply a fixed order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( يخرجه عن كونه حداً لأن الحد ما يكون بالذاتيات فقط، إما كلها أو بعضها . قوله: (يكون بينهما ترتيب قد يمنع بعد تسليم الاحتياج إلى جزئية القرينة، بل إلى نفسها أيضاً وجوب الترتيب بينهما وبينها بل هو من المواضع التي يوجد فيها التركيب، والتأليف بدون الترتيب .
His statement: 'It removes it from being a definition because the definition is only by essential (dhati) things, either all or some of them.' His statement: 'There is arrangement between them' — it may be denied that, after conceding the need for the part of the indicator, or even for itself, the obligation of arrangement between them and it is necessary; rather, it is among the cases where composition and combination exist without arrangement.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
The commentator clarifies two points from the original argument. First: adding an indicator removes the definition from being a proper limit (hadd) because a definition must consist only of essential (dhati) attributes, either all (complete definition) or some (incomplete definition). Second: the claim that there must be arrangement between the parts is rejected; even if we accept the need for an indicator, arrangement is not necessary. Composition (tarkīb) and combination (ta’līf) can exist without arrangement (tartīb), as seen in some cases.

Key Terms
الحد
الذاتيات
التركيب
التأليف
الترتيب
القرينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any composition must have arrangement between its parts.
Correction: Composition can exist without arrangement, as shown by the commentator's example: 'composition and combination exist without arrangement'.

Checkpoint
What is the condition for something to be a definition (hadd) according to the text?
A) It must include both essential and accidental attributes.
B) It must consist only of essential (dhati) attributes, either all or some.
C) It must include a mental indicator.
D) It must have arrangement between its parts.

Key Takeaway
A definition must consist only of essential attributes; composition does not require arrangement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you define your faith to yourself or others, do you focus on essential attributes (like tawhid and prophethood) or do you mix in non-essential cultural customs or personal preferences that may not be required? Are you willing to strip away the non-essential to preserve a pure definition?
?
Is there an area of your religious practice where you insist on a specific order or arrangement that God has not made obligatory? Examine whether that insistence stems from the essence of the practice or from habit—what would change if you allowed flexibility?
?
This lesson shows that composition can exist without arrangement. Consider your spiritual life: do you have many religious activities (prayer, charity, study) that are composed but lack proper priority and order? What one change could you make to align your actions with essential truths rather than mere accumulation?

Universal Accident Cannot Be Part of Differentia
Opening assertion: universal accident excluded from differentia

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the universal accident cannot be included in the concept of the differentia.

Core Idea
The universal accident must be excluded from the differentia, or it ceases to be a differentia.

The commentator begins by addressing a crucial logical point: the universal accident (al-‘ard al-‘amm) cannot be considered part of the differentia (fasl). This is a foundational rule in definition theory. The passage asserts this as a categorical prohibition, and the lesson explores the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening assertion
Read the Arabic sentence stating that the universal accident is absolutely not allowed in the concept of differentia.
Note the word 'qaṭ‘an' (absolutely) emphasizing certainty.
2
Understand the consequence
If it were allowed, it would not be a differentia. Reflect on why this logical exclusion is necessary for a proper definition.
3
Connect to broader context
Consider how this rule fits into the discussion of non-exclusivity of definition by singular terms.
This is part of a larger argument about what can constitute a definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وأما الثاني فلعدم انحصار إلخ) وأيضاً العرض العام لا يجوز اعتباره في مفهوم الفصل قطعاً، وإلا لم يكن فصلاً ولو أريد بالشيء ذاته، لزم انقلاب مادة الإمكان الخاص في مثل : قولنا الإنسان ضاحك بالامكان الخاص إلى الضرورة لأن ثبوت الشيء لنفسه ضروري لا يقال : المعتبر في حال الحمل هو المفهوم، وفي حال التحديد هو الذات فيندفع المحذور لانا نقول : الكلام في الأمر الذي اعتبره الواضع في مفهوم المشتق، ولا شك أن الواضح لم يعتبر حال الحمل وصفاً، وفي حالة التعريف وصفاً ،آخر قيل : إذا أريد بالمشتق المشتق حقيقة أو حكماً كما ذكروا في الخبر والحال كان منحصراً وفيه نظر لأن هذا إنما يتم إذا لزم تأويل الجامد الواقع في التعريف بالمشتق لزومه فيهما ، وهو أول المسألة اللهم إلا أن يبني الكلام على أنه يجب أن يصح جعل المعرف خبراً عن المعرف، ومحمولاً عليه وإن لم يكن بينهما حكم بالفعل وفيه ما فيه .
His statement: 'As for the second, because of non-exclusivity...' etc. — Also, the universal accident is absolutely not allowed to be considered in the concept of differentia, otherwise it would not be a differentia. Even if one intends by 'thing' (al-shay') its essence, it would necessitate the conversion of the matter of special possibility in such as our saying 'man is a risible being' by special possibility into necessity, because the affirmation of a thing for itself is necessary. It cannot be said: what is considered in the case of predication is the concept, and in the case of definition is the essence, so the problem is repelled. For we say: the discussion concerns the matter that the positor considered in the concept of the derived term, and there is no doubt that the positor did not consider in the case of predication a description, and in the case of definition a description. Another said: if by derived term is meant the derived term in reality or by judgement, as they mentioned in the case of خبر and حال, then it would be confined, and there is consideration in this, because this only holds if the interpretation of the noun (al-jāmid) occurring in the definition as a derived term is necessary in both, and that is the beginning of the issue. Unless one bases the statement on the necessity that the definer (al-mu‘arrif) can be made a predicate of the defined (al-mu‘arraf) and predicated on it, even if there is no actual judgment between them, and there is what there is in that.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a complex commentary on the second point about the non-exclusivity of definition by singular terms. The commentator argues that the universal accident (al-‘ard al-‘amm) should not be considered part of the differentia, otherwise it would not be a differentia. If 'thing' (shay') refers to the essence itself, then in a statement like 'man is a risible being', the possibility becomes necessity because self-affirmation is necessary. The commentator rejects a potential objection that predication considers the concept while definition considers the essence, arguing that the positor (wāḍi‘) did not consider different descriptions in predication and definition. Another argument is mentioned: if by derived term we mean real or virtual derivation, then definition by singular would be confined to derived terms, but this requires interpreting the noun (jāmid) in the definition as derived, which is debatable. The final point is that the definer must be able to be predicated of the defined even without actual judgment.

Key Terms
العرض العام
فصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that any shared property can be part of the differentia if it is distinctive in context.
Correction: The universal accident, by definition, is shared across multiple species, so it cannot serve as a differentia, which must be unique to a species.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator say about the universal accident (al-‘ard al-‘amm) in the concept of differentia?
A) It is sometimes allowed.
B) It is absolutely not allowed.
C) It is necessary for definition.
D) It is optional.

Key Takeaway
The universal accident must be excluded from the differentia; otherwise, it ceases to be a differentia.

Conversion of Possibility to Necessity in Self-Affirmation
Reductio argument: possibility becomes necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that if the universal accident is considered in the differentia, the mode of possibility becomes necessity due to self-affirmation.

Core Idea
If the universal accident were part of the differentia, possibility becomes necessity because a thing's self-affirmation is necessary.

The commentator presents a logical consequence: if one tries to include the universal accident in the differentia by interpreting 'thing' as essence, then a statement like 'man is risible' (which expresses special possibility) would become necessary, because a thing's affirmation of itself is necessary. This is a reductio ad absurdum to prove the exclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditional argument
Examine the sentence starting with 'Wa law urīda...' which sets up the hypothetical scenario.
The example given is 'man is risible' (al-insānu ḍāḥik).
2
Identify the logical transformation
The argument says that if the universal accident were included by taking 'thing' as essence, then the special possibility (al-imkān al-khāṣṣ) would turn into necessity (al-ḍarūra).
The reason: 'thubūt al-shay’ li-nafsihi ḍarūrī' (the affirmation of a thing for itself is necessary).
3
Understand the reductio
This transformation is undesirable because it changes the modal status, showing that the initial assumption leads to contradiction.
Thus, the universal accident cannot be part of the differentia.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وأما الثاني فلعدم انحصار إلخ) وأيضاً العرض العام لا يجوز اعتباره في مفهوم الفصل قطعاً، وإلا لم يكن فصلاً ولو أريد بالشيء ذاته، لزم انقلاب مادة الإمكان الخاص في مثل : قولنا الإنسان ضاحك بالامكان الخاص إلى الضرورة لأن ثبوت الشيء لنفسه ضروري لا يقال : المعتبر في حال الحمل هو المفهوم، وفي حال التحديد هو الذات فيندفع المحذور لانا نقول : الكلام في الأمر الذي اعتبره الواضع في مفهوم المشتق، ولا شك أن الواضح لم يعتبر حال الحمل وصفاً، وفي حالة التعريف وصفاً ،آخر قيل : إذا أريد بالمشتق المشتق حقيقة أو حكماً كما ذكروا في الخبر والحال كان منحصراً وفيه نظر لأن هذا إنما يتم إذا لزم تأويل الجامد الواقع في التعريف بالمشتق لزومه فيهما ، وهو أول المسألة اللهم إلا أن يبني الكلام على أنه يجب أن يصح جعل المعرف خبراً عن المعرف، ومحمولاً عليه وإن لم يكن بينهما حكم بالفعل وفيه ما فيه .
His statement: 'As for the second, because of non-exclusivity...' etc. — Also, the universal accident is absolutely not allowed to be considered in the concept of differentia, otherwise it would not be a differentia. Even if one intends by 'thing' (al-shay') its essence, it would necessitate the conversion of the matter of special possibility in such as our saying 'man is a risible being' by special possibility into necessity, because the affirmation of a thing for itself is necessary. Unless one bases the statement on the necessity that the definer (al-mu‘arrif) can be made a predicate of the defined (al-mu‘arraf) and predicated on it, even if there is no actual judgment between them, and there is what there is in that.

Commentary
This passage is a complex commentary on the second point about the non-exclusivity of definition by singular terms. The commentator argues that the universal accident (al-‘ard al-‘amm) should not be considered part of the differentia, otherwise it would not be a differentia. Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to the special possibility in 'man is risible' if the universal accident is considered part of the differentia by taking 'thing' as essence?
A) It remains possibility.
B) It becomes necessity.
C) It becomes impossible.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you define yourself, do you include things you do (actions) as part of your essence? How would that make those actions necessary rather than chosen? Is that how Allah sees you, or are your deeds separate from your identity as a believer?
?
If you treat a sin as part of your identity ('I am an angry person'), does that make anger necessary and unchangeable? How does this lesson's principle—that accidents cannot be part of the essence—give you hope for change?
?
Consider a statement like 'I am a servant of Allah.' Is it possible or necessary for you? If it is necessary (because it is your true essence), what does that demand of you in your daily life? Are you living as if it were necessary or just possible?

Objection and Response on Predication vs. Definition
Objection and response on predication vs definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that predication considers concept while definition considers essence, and the commentator’s response regarding the positor’s intention.

Core Idea
The objection that predication and definition consider different things fails because the positor had a unified intention.

After presenting the reductio, the commentator anticipates an objection: perhaps the problem is avoided because in predication we consider the concept, while in definition we consider the essence. The commentator responds by stating that the discussion is about what the positor (wāḍi‘) intended in the concept of the derived term, and the positor did not assign different descriptions for predication and definition. This lesson examines that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection says: 'lā yuqāl: al-mu‘tabar fī ḥāl al-ḥaml huwa al-mafhūm, wa fī ḥāl al-taḥdīd huwa al-dhāt' (what is considered in predication is the concept, in definition the essence).
This objection tries to dissolve the earlier reductio.
2
Read the response
The commentator responds: 'al-kalām fī al-amr alladhī i‘tabarahu al-wāḍi‘... wa lā shakk anna al-wāḍi‘ lam ya‘tabir fī ḥāl al-ḥaml waṣfan wa fī ḥāl al-ta‘rīf waṣfan' (the discussion concerns what the positor considered; he did not consider a different description for each case).
The positor’s intention is unified; the distinction is irrelevant.
3
Reflect on the implications
Understand why the positor's perspective is decisive in this logical argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وأما الثاني فلعدم انحصار إلخ) وأيضاً العرض العام لا يجوز اعتباره في مفهوم الفصل قطعاً، وإلا لم يكن فصلاً ولو أريد بالشيء ذاته، لزم انقلاب مادة الإمكان الخاص في مثل : قولنا الإنسان ضاحك بالامكان الخاص إلى الضرورة لأن ثبوت الشيء لنفسه ضروري لا يقال : المعتبر في حال الحمل هو المفهوم، وفي حال التحديد هو الذات فيندفع المحذور لانا نقول : الكلام في الأمر الذي اعتبره الواضع في مفهوم المشتق، ولا شك أن الواضح لم يعتبر حال الحمل وصفاً، وفي حالة التعريف وصفاً ،آخر قيل : إذا أريد بالمشتق المشتق حقيقة أو حكماً كما ذكروا في الخبر والحال كان منحصراً وفيه نظر لأن هذا إنما يتم إذا لزم تأويل الجامد الواقع في التعريف بالمشتق لزومه فيهما ، وهو أول المسألة اللهم إلا أن يبني الكلام على أنه يجب أن يصح جعل المعرف خبراً عن المعرف، ومحمولاً عليه وإن لم يكن بينهما حكم بالفعل وفيه ما فيه .

The commentator argues that the universal accident (al-‘ard al-‘amm) should not be considered part of the differentia, otherwise it would not be a differentia. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the meaning of a term shifts between predication and definition.
Correction: The positor assigned a single meaning, so the term is used with the same concept in both contexts.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why does the objection that predication considers concept and definition considers essence fail?
A) Because predication and definition are the same.
B) Because the positor had a unified consideration of the term.

D) Because the reductio is about a different issue.

Key Takeaway
The positor’s unified intention for the derived term means that the distinction between predication and definition does not resolve the contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself using a term one way in your own thinking and another way when explaining it to others? How does that affect the consistency of your understanding before Allah?
?
The positor had a single intention for the derived term—not splitting its meaning by context. When you speak about matters of faith, is your intention unified, or do you subtly shift meanings to avoid difficulty?
?
If your words were judged by the same fixed intention you hold, would your statements about Allah's attributes be consistent? What does that require of you today?

Argument on Derivation Confinement and Predicability Requirement
Discussion on confinement through derivation and predicability condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the final arguments: if confinement requires interpreting nouns as derived, and the condition that the definer must be predicable of the defined.

Core Idea
Even if confinement holds, it forces reinterpretation of nouns, and the definer must be predicable of the defined.

The commentator addresses another view: if by 'derived term' we mean real or virtual derivation, then definition by singular would be confined to derived terms. But he notes this only works if we reinterpret every noun (jāmid) in a definition as derived, which is questionable. Finally, a condition is added: the definer must be predicable of the defined, even without an actual judgment. This lesson explores these finishing points.

Guided Path
1
Read the second view
Read: 'ākhar qīla: idhā urīda bil-mushtaqq al-mushtaqq ḥaqīqatan aw ḥukman... kāna munḥaṣiran' (if by derived term is meant real or virtual derivation, it would be confined).
Note the terms 'khābar' and 'ḥāl' used as examples.
2
Understand the critical response
The commentator says 'fīhi naẓar' (there is consideration) because it requires interpreting the noun (jāmid) as derived, which is contentious.
3
Examine the final condition
The last part states: 'yajibu an yaṣiḥḥa ja‘l al-mu‘arrif khabaran ‘an al-mu‘arraf wa maḥmūlan ‘alayhi' (it must be possible to make the definer a predicate of the defined).
Even if no actual judgment exists between them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وأما الثاني فلعدم انحصار إلخ) وأيضاً العرض العام لا يجوز اعتباره في مفهوم الفصل قطعاً، وإلا لم يكن فصلاً ولو أريد بالشيء ذاته، لزم انقلاب مادة الإمكان الخاص في مثل : قولنا الإنسان ضاحك بالامكان الخاص إلى الضرورة لأن ثبوت الشيء لنفسه ضروري لا يقال : المعتبر في حال الحمل هو المفهوم، وفي حال التحديد هو الذات فيندفع المحذور لانا نقول : الكلام في الأمر الذي اعتبره الواضع في مفهوم المشتق، ولا شك أن الواضح لم يعتبر حال الحمل وصفاً، وفي حالة التعريف وصفاً ،آخر قيل : إذا أريد بالمشتق المشتق حقيقة أو حكماً كما ذكروا في الخبر والحال كان منحصراً وفيه نظر لأن هذا إنما يتم إذا لزم تأويل الجامد الواقع في التعريف بالمشتق لزومه فيهما ، وهو أول المسألة اللهم إلا أن يبني الكلام على أنه يجب أن يصح جعل المعرف خبراً عن المعرف، ومحمولاً عليه وإن لم يكن بينهما حكم بالفعل وفيه ما فيه .
His statement: 'As for the second, because of non-exclusivity...' etc. — Also, the universal accident is absolutely not allowed to be considered in the concept of differentia, otherwise it would not be a differentia. Even if one intends by 'thing' (al-shay') its essence, it would necessitate the conversion of the matter of special possibility in such as our saying 'man is a risible being' by special possibility into necessity, because the affirmation of a thing for itself is necessary. It cannot be said: what is considered in the case of predication is the concept, and in the case of definition is the essence, so the problem is repelled. For we say: the discussion concerns the matter that the positor considered in the concept of the derived term, and there is no doubt that the positor did not consider in the case of predication a description, and in the case of definition a description. Another said: if by derived term is meant the derived term in reality or by judgement, as they mentioned in the case of خبر and حال, then it would be confined, and there is consideration in this, because this only holds if the interpretation of the noun (al-jāmid) occurring in the definition as a derived term is necessary in both, and that is the beginning of the issue. Unless one bases the statement on the necessity that the definer (al-mu‘arrif) can be made a predicate of the defined (al-mu‘arraf) and predicated on it, even if there is no actual judgment between them, and there is what there is in that.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a complex commentary on the second point about the non-exclusivity of definition by singular terms. The commentator argues that the universal accident (al-‘ard al-‘amm) should not be considered part of the differentia, otherwise it would not be a differentia. If 'thing' (shay') refers to the essence itself, then in a statement like 'man is a risible being', the possibility becomes necessity because self-affirmation is necessary. The commentator rejects a potential objection that predication considers the concept while definition considers the essence, arguing that the positor (wāḍi‘) did not consider different descriptions in predication and definition. Another argument is mentioned: if by derived term we mean real or virtual derivation, then definition by singular would be confined to derived terms, but this requires interpreting the noun (jāmid) in the definition as derived, which is debatable. The final point is that the definer must be able to be predicated of the defined even without actual judgment.
